Discussion Name: Should the Primary Focus of Prisons be Rehabilitation, or Punishment?

1. The primary focus of prisons should be rehabilitation and reintegration, not punishment.
1.1. Pro: Rehabilitation [reduces the likelihood of a criminal reoffending](https://law.jrank.org/pages/1936/Rehabilitation-Does-correctional-rehabilitation-work.html)(In contrast, it is now apparent that rehabilitation programs generally reduce recidivism and, when conducted according to the "principles of effective treatment" \(Gendreau\), cut reoffending substantially \(Andrews and Bonta; Cullen and Gendreau; Lipsey and Wilson; Lurigio\). Evidence favorable to rehabilitation has been generated by a statistical technique called meta-analysis. Traditionally, criminologists such as Martinson would read over a group of studies evaluating treatment programs. They would then either describe what the studies found—a narrative review—or try to count how many studies showed that offender treatment worked or did not work—the "ballot box" method. A meta-analysis, however, essentially computes a batting average across all studies, calculating the average impact of treatment on recidivism. Using this method, the existing research, which now involves hundreds of evaluation studies, shows that rehabilitation programs reduce recidivism about 10 percentage points.), providing the greatest benefit to the community.
1.1.1. Pro: Many countries seem to have reduced recidivism rates by focusing on rehabilitation in prisons.
1.1.1.1. Pro: In the Netherlands, where the prison system [focuses heavily on rehabilitation](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2019/dec/12/why-are-there-so-few-prisoners-in-the-netherlands)(Why are there so few prisoners in the Netherlands?\n\nThe Dutch justice system is cutting jail populations by offering specialist rehabilitation to people with mental illnesses\n\n\[...\]\n\nToday plummeting prison sentences have left the Netherlands with an unusual problem: it doesn’t have enough inmates to fill its prisons, even after renting out places to Norway and Belgium.\n\nSince 2014, 23 prisons have been shut, turning into temporary asylum centres, housing and hotels. The country has Europe’s third-lowest incarceration rate, at 54.4 per 100,000 inhabitants.), there has been such a large decline in prison population that they have started accepting prisoners from [other countries](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2015/sep/01/norwegian-inmates-sent-to-dutch-prison-cells-too-full)(Norway has sent a group of inmates to a jail in the Netherlands because its own prisons are too full.\n\n“The first prisoners have arrived,” Karl Hillesland, the Norwegian head of Norgerhaven prison in the Netherlands said on Tuesday.\n\nDue to lack of space, more than 1,000 inmates in Norway are waiting to be placed in prisons, where they are often assigned to individual cells. To solve the problem, Norway has leased Norgerhaven prison from the Netherlands.).
1.1.1.2. Con: Some countries with rehabilitative prison systems still incarcerate dangerous prisoners for extended periods.
1.1.1.2.1. Pro: The mass murderer responsible for the 2011 terrorist attack in Oslo was sentenced to [21 years for his crimes](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2022/jan/18/norwegian-mass-killer-anders-breivik-begins-parole-hearing)(Far-right Norwegian terrorist, who murdered 77 people, trying for parole after 10 years in jail\n\nThe mass killer, who has served less than half of his 21-year sentence, has shown no remorse over the massacre and could use the hearing as an opportunity to voice his extreme views, experts say.) and [may never be released](https://www.thejournal.ie/21-years-breivik-trial-sentence-norway-570722-Aug2012/)(A sentence of permanent detention can be imposed if there is considerable danger of repetition. Permanent detention is not subject to any timeframe. However, the court always fixes a timeframe that may not exceed 21 years. When the timeframe expires the offender may be re-assessed. If the court concludes that there is still a danger of repetition the timeframe may be extended by up to five years at a time. There is no upper limit to the number of times that the court may extend the timeframe.).
1.1.1.2.1.1. Con: The [worst individual mass murderer](https://www.worldatlas.com/articles/the-deadliest-mass-shootings-in-history.html)(1 Garissa University College Attack Garissa, Kenya 2015 148\n2 Peshawar School Massacre Peshawar, Pakistan 2014 149\n3 November 2015 Paris Attacks Paris, France 2015 130\n4 2011 Norway Attacks Oslo, Norway 2011 77) in history is not a reasonable example of a common exception to the rule.
1.1.1.2.2. Pro: Sweden expanded the ability for courts to [hand down life sentences](https://www.thelocal.se/20190605/swedish-laws-are-about-to-get-tougher-on-murder/)(The law change will mean that more external factors can be considered for a life sentence.\n\n“It could be that the perpetrator caused the victim severe suffering, that the method was particularly reckless, that a person close to the perpetrator was the target, that it happened in the victim's home or in front of other people close to them, for example children,” explained Sweden's Justice Minister Morgan Johansson, adding that two thirds of female murder victims are killed by someone close to them.\n\nA majority of convicted murderers will be sentenced to life in jail after the new law comes into effect, according to the minister, with the percentage of life sentences then set to increase from 30 to over 50.) to murderers in 2020.
1.1.1.2.3. Con: Alternatively, there are some prisons in punishment-oriented countries that [focus on rehabilitation](https://www.al.com/news/2022/02/alabama-paroles-bureau-buys-private-prison-in-perry-county-to-use-for-rehabilitation.html)(The Alabama Bureau of Pardons and Paroles has purchased a vacant, private prison in Perry County to use as a rehabilitation facility for parole violators.\n\nBureau Director Cam Ward said the bureau will pay $15 million for the Perry County Correctional Center, a 700-bed prison on U.S. 80 in Uniontown. The prison was owned by GEO Group, a private prison company. It will be called the Paroles Reentry Center. The contract was signed Tuesday.).
1.1.1.3. Con: Statistics on recidivism are hard to compare, as many countries use different criteria to measure the rates at which criminals reoffend \([p. 2](http://www.salve.edu/sites/default/files/filesfield/documents/Incarceration_and_Recidivism.pdf)(It should be noted that comparing international recidivism rates can be tricky. Countries track them differently, often using different terms \(reconviction, re-arrest, relapse, re-imprisonment\) and varied lengths of time for studies \(1 yr, 3 yrs, 10 yrs\).)\).
1.1.1.4. Pro: Nordic prisons, many of which primarily focus on rehabilitation, have low recidivism rates \([p. 1](https://encompass.eku.edu/cgi/viewcontent.cgi?article=1680&context=honors_theses)(While countries in Scandinavia have an incarceration rate averaging only 75/100,000 people, the rate in the U.S. is more than 700/100,000. Scandinavian countries have the lowest recidivism rate in the world: less than 20%, and the United States has a recidivism rate of 70% \(Deady, 2014\).)\).
1.1.1.4.1. Pro: Sweden’s focus on rehabilitation resulted in such a low recidivism rate \([40%](https://www.theguardian.com/society/2014/nov/26/prison-sweden-not-punishment-nils-oberg)(With reoffending rates at about 40% – less than half of those in the UK and most other European countries – does he attribute this success to the country’s effective policies on prisoner rehabilitation? “We obviously believe that it is part of the explanation; we hope we are doing something right. But it’s going to be very difficult to prove that scientifically. We are increasing our efforts all the time,” he says.)\) that it had to [close four prisons](https://www.theguardian.com/society/2013/dec/01/why-sweden-closing-prisons)(Why is Sweden closing its prisons?\nSweden's prison population has dropped so dramatically that the country plans to close four of its prisons. What lessons can the UK learn?) in 2013.
1.1.1.4.1.1. Pro: Nearly all prison terms in Sweden are less than ten years. Even life sentences effectively mean that felons will only be in prison until a judge decides that [they have been rehabilitated](https://www.theguardian.com/society/2013/dec/01/why-sweden-closing-prisons)(jail terms rarely exceed 10 years; those who receive life imprisonment can still apply to the courts after a decade to have the sentence commuted to a fixed term, usually in the region of 18 to 25 years. Sweden was the first country in Europe to introduce the electronic tagging of convicted criminals and continues to strive to minimise short-term prison sentences wherever possible by using community-based measures – proven to be more effective at reducing reoffending.).
1.1.1.4.1.2. Con: Sweden's recidivism rate of 40% is still [double](https://theworld.org/stories/2015-04-15/finlands-open-prisons-inmates-have-keys)(“The lesson from Finland was that it was perfectly possible to drop the use of imprisonment \[by two-thirds,\]” he says, “and that did not disturb the crime trend development in Finland.”\n\nWhat did work was a gradual reintroduction into normal life, the kind that the open prisons offer. About a third of Finnish inmates are housed in open prison, and Finland’s Criminal Sanctions Agency says inmates who go through open prisons are less likely to be arrested again. The reoffending rate drops almost 20 percent.) that of neighboring Finland, where one-third of the inmates cohabitate with the guards in open prisons.
1.1.1.4.2. Con: Nordic countries have [smaller populations](https://data.worldbank.org/indicator/AG.SRF.TOTL.K2)(Rank Country 2022 Population 2021 Population Growth Rate Area Density \(km²\) \n\n\[...\]\n\n92 Sweden 10,218,971 10,160,169 0.58% 450,295 23/km²\n\n\[...\]\n\n116 Denmark 5,834,950 5,813,298 0.37% 43,094 135/km²\n\n\[...\]\n\n118 Finland 5,554,960 5,548,360 0.12% 338,424 16/km²\n\n119 Norway 5,511,370 5,465,630 0.84% 323,802 17/km²) and are [wealthier](https://worldhappiness.report/ed/2020/the-nordic-exceptionalism-what-explains-why-the-nordic-countries-are-constantly-among-the-happiest-in-the-world/)(To examine this issue, we take a look at the Gallup World Poll data as regards these factors. Given that the Nordic countries are all relatively rich \(Nordic countries occupy a range from 6 \(Norway\) to 21 \(Finland\) in the 149-country ranking of GDP per capita\), we are especially interested what factors beyond GDP per capita make the Nordic countries stand out.) than most other countries. These inherent advantages make comparisons with other countries unproductive.
1.1.1.4.3. Con: Most Nordic countries are [welfare states](https://slate.com/news-and-politics/2014/08/will-everyone-shut-up-already-about-how-the-nordic-countries-top-every-global-ranking.html)(They exist within a broader societal context. The small gender gap, the chance for all students to succeed in school, the respect for the dignity of the homeless, paid leave—all of this exists because the Nordic countries are, proudly, welfare states.\n\nEvery one of the aforementioned policies exists because it is part of a welfare state, and because, in the Nordic countries unlike in America, there is no shame \(and, in fact, quite a lot of pride\) in being a welfare state. There are many who think that we, too, should move to a welfare model. However, in a country wherein healthcare is deemed “a part of a socialist vision for America” \(and wherein that is necessarily understood to be a bad thing\), the establishment of this kind of political system does not seem very likely.). Therefore, their low recidivism rates are not a result of specific penal policies but a consequence of the broader political system which enables them.
1.1.1.4.4. Pro: Norway’s [low crime rate](https://www.oecdbetterlifeindex.org/topics/safety/)(Personal security is a core element for the well-being of individuals. Do you feel safe out walking, alone at night, for example? In Norway, about 93% of people say that they feel safe walking alone at night, much more than the OECD average of 74%, and the highest in the OECD.\n\nThe homicide rate \(the number of murders per 100 000 inhabitants\) is a more reliable measure of a country's safety level because, unlike other crimes, murders are usually always reported to the police. According to the latest OECD data, Norway's homicide rate is 0.6, less than the OECD average of 2.6.) shows that the concept of [restorative justice](https://www.businessinsider.com/why-norways-prison-system-is-so-successful-2014-12?r=US&IR=T)(Norway also has a relatively low level of crime compared to the US, according to the Bureau of Diplomatic Security. The majority of crimes reported to police there are theft-related incidents, and violent crime is mostly confined to areas with drug trafficking and gang problems. \n\nBased on that information, it's safe to assume Norway's criminal justice system is doing something right. Few citizens there go to prison, and those who do usually go only once. So how does Norway accomplish this feat? The country relies on a concept called "restorative justice," which aims to repair the harm caused by crime rather than punish people. This system focuses on rehabilitating prisoners.), which focuses on rehabilitating prisoners, is an effective means of reducing criminality.
1.1.1.4.4.1. Pro: [Recidivism](https://nij.ojp.gov/topics/corrections/recidivism)(Recidivism is one of the most fundamental concepts in criminal justice. It refers to a person's relapse into criminal behavior, often after the person receives sanctions or undergoes intervention for a previous crime.) in American prisons, which focus on punishment, is nearly twice as high as recidivism in Norwegian prisons, which focus on rehabilitation \([p. 4](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC4472929/pdf/pone.0130390.pdf)(Reconviction rates\n\n\[...\]\n\nNorway 2005 Prisoners 2 years 20%\n\n\[...\]\n\nUS 2005–2010 Prisoners 2 years \n36%)\).
1.1.1.4.4.1.1. Con: Norway is a [fairly homogeneous society](https://www.wallstwatchdog.com/money-career/3-reasons-why-norways-prison-system-should-not-be-replicated-in-america/)(Norway is a fairly homogeneous society with a shared history and culture. In contrast, the United States is a cacophony of cultures and immigrants. There is constant friction between established groups and society’s reinvention by new batches of immigrants.) compared to the US, making comparisons between the two less valuable.
1.1.1.4.4.1.1.1. Pro: Norway is predominantly [white European](https://worldpopulationreview.com/countries/norway-population)(The ethnicities in Norway are recorded as Norwegian 83.2% \(includes about 60,000 Sami\), other European 8.3%, other 8.5% by the Factbook as well.), while the US has a [large African American population](https://www.pewresearch.org/social-trends/fact-sheet/facts-about-the-us-black-population/)(There were 46.8 million people in the U.S. who identified as Black in 2019. The Black population has grown by more than 10 million since 2000, when 36.2 million of the U.S. population identified as Black, marking a 29% increase over almost two decades.\n\nMore than 4.6 million Black people in the U.S. were born outside the country as of 2019, meaning that 10% of the Black population was foreign born. This is an increase from 2000, when 2.4 million people, or roughly 7%, among the Black population were foreign born.) due to its historical legacy of slavery \([p. 12](https://extranet.sioe.org/uploads/sioe2016/gouda_rigterink.pdf)(Estimates suggest that an increase in the slave population from 0% to 15.6% \(the mean level in the sample\) is associated with an additional 0.12 instances of violent crime per 100,000 population, or an increase in violent crime of between 1.5% and 2.2%, depending on the base line level of crime in the state at hand. Increasing the slave population by two standard deviations from its mean level results in an estimated increase in violent crime of 0.32 instances per 100,000 population, representing an increase in the range of 4.2% to 6%. This is in line with our overall hypothesis regarding the relation between slavery and violent crime. Note that while we find that this relation persists across the decades, we cannot really strictly compare the estimated coefficients due to the differences in the numbers of observations between the years.)\).
1.1.1.4.4.1.1.2. Con: The population of Norway is more diverse than at any point in history, with immigration accounting for [72% of population growth](https://www.ssb.no/en/befolkning/artikler-og-publikasjoner/large-diversity-in-little-norway)(The ethnic and cultural diversity in Norway is greater now than ever before. The population grew 1.3 per cent last year, with immigration accounting for 72 per cent of this growth. This means that the country’s population, which has passed the 5 million mark, is now much more diverse. Equally diverse are the reasons for persons born in other countries embarking on the long or short journey to Norway with hopes of living and working here. Their children, with their multi-cultural backgrounds, will also gradually make their mark on the country.).
1.1.1.4.4.1.1.3. Con: Immigrants make up about [19%](https://www.ssb.no/en/befolkning/innvandrere/statistikk/innvandrere-og-norskfodte-med-innvandrerforeldre)(Immigrants and Norwegian-born to immigrant parents in per cent of total population\n18.9) of the population of Norway. In the US, the rate stands at about [14%](https://www.migrationpolicy.org/article/frequently-requested-statistics-immigrants-and-immigration-united-states)(More than 44.9 million immigrants lived in the United States in 2019, the historical numeric high since census records have been kept. In 2019, immigrants comprised 13.7 percent of the total U.S. population, a figure that remains short of the record high of 14.8 percent in 1890.), making the two countries relatively comparable.
1.1.1.4.4.1.1.3.1. Con: The proportion of immigrants in the Norwegian prison system is [much higher](https://www.wallstwatchdog.com/money-career/3-reasons-why-norways-prison-system-should-not-be-replicated-in-america/)(The Times story observes that a large number of current inmates in Norway’s prison system are immigrants. In fact, the share of foreign prisoners in Norway’s prison system has been steadily increasing over the years. That increase has also coincided with an increase in the number of immigrants to Norway.\n\nCompare that to the United States, where only 6% of the prison population is from abroad. Extrapolating past data, it may be reasonable to conclude that as the number of immigrants seeking asylum in Norway increases due to its welfare model, the number of foreign prisoners may also increase.) than in the US because undocumented immigrants in the US are [far less likely](https://www.pnas.org/doi/10.1073/pnas.2014704117)(Contrary to public perception, we observe considerably lower felony arrest rates among undocumented immigrants compared to legal immigrants and native-born US citizens and find no evidence that undocumented criminality has increased in recent years. Our findings help us understand why the most aggressive immigrant removal programs have not delivered on their crime reduction promises and are unlikely to do so in the future.) to commit crimes.
1.1.1.4.4.1.2. Con: Economic models between the US and Norway are [vastly different](https://www.wallstwatchdog.com/money-career/3-reasons-why-norways-prison-system-should-not-be-replicated-in-america/)(First, the two countries work on different economic paradigms. Much like other Scandinavian countries, Norway subscribes to the social welfare state model. In this model, the state levies high taxes from a mostly rich population and, in return, provides a number of social services, ranging from day care to paternity leave.\n\nAs a result of the broad public agreement between the country’s citizens about social welfare, the government has considerable leeway and freedom to pursue radical experimentation in providing social services.\n\nIn contrast, the American economic system is a melange of capitalism, libertarianism, and restricted social welfare. This system is best reflected in the constant tension between pure libertarianism and government intervention in America.), which renders the two incomparable.
1.1.1.4.4.1.2.1. Pro: As a result of their economic models, inequality in the US is [much higher](https://borgenproject.org/norway-quality-of-life-far-exceeds-america/)(The United States has a lower per capita GDP than Norway with a GDP of 51,749 compared to 99,558, respectively, and is also home to one of the most pressing income distribution gaps in any industrialized nation, surpassed in income inequality by only Russia and Mexico.\n\nDue to America’s cavernous income inequality, the poorest 38% of Norwegians are better off than the poorest 38% of Americans despite an overall lower average per capita GDP. According to Syracuse University professor Timothy Smeeding, the United States relies heavily on the markets to an extent that social safety nets are neglected, unlike Norway, which focuses more resources on providing aid to the poor.) than in Norway, which causes a [higher crime rate](https://www.theatlantic.com/business/archive/2014/10/does-inequality-cause-crime/381748/)(Strain theory suggests that when poorer people perceive inequality, they feel less of a commitment to social norms and in turn come to view crime as more acceptable. The key insight the Hicks’s study provides is that when potential criminals are giving up on social expectations, they’re doing so based on information that’s visible, not information that’s password-protected.).
1.1.1.4.4.1.2.2. Con: Pointing out all the differences between the two countries is not conducive to adapting some of the potential teachings from Norway for application in the US prison system.
1.1.1.4.4.1.3. Pro: Several US states are [implementing Norwegian-style prison reforms](https://www.detroitnews.com/story/news/special-reports/2019/10/11/states-put-norway-style-prison-reforms-to-work/1682876001/)(U.S. prison officials and academics are increasingly looking to Norway's approach to incarceration for ideas to reduce recidivism rates that are higher in the United States than anywhere else in the world. \n\nNorth Dakota and Oregon have been front-runners in implementing Norwegian-style reforms, said Brie Williams, director of Amend: Changing Correctional Culture, a nonprofit based at the University of California San Francisco.) in hopes of reducing recidivism.
1.1.1.4.4.1.4. Pro: Louisiana, the state with the highest incarceration rate in the US, implemented a reentry court to provide prisoners with the highest certifications available in their chosen vocation. These simple steps have reduced recidivism to [a fraction of the state's overall 50% recidivism rate](https://thehill.com/opinion/criminal-justice/379814-the-criminal-justice-systems-top-job-breaking-the-cycle-of-crime/)(Louisiana, the state with the highest incarceration rate in the country, implemented a program known as “reentry court” in 2010. This innovative approach combines mentoring, substance abuse treatment, and intensive vocational training. Those participating in this program are permitted to obtain the highest level of certifications in their chosen vocation while serving their sentences. With this qualification in hand, participants then must obtain a job offer before they can be released to an intensive reentry program. Early outcomes have shown recidivism rates at a fraction of the almost 50 percent failure rate among others who exit prison in Louisiana.) for the unrehabilitated prisoners.
1.1.1.4.4.2. Pro: The [incarceration rate](https://www.businessinsider.com.au/why-norways-prison-system-is-so-successful-2014-12?r=UK&IR=T)(That makes Norway's incarceration rate just 75 per 100,000 people, compared to 707 people for every 100,000 people in the US.) in Norway is just 75 per 100,000 people compared to 707 per 100,000 people in the US.
1.1.1.4.4.3. Con: The low crime rate in Norway may be due to other factors such as a [comprehensive welfare system](https://www.huffpost.com/entry/norway-best-country-to-live_n_5a6059c7e4b046f0811d0235)(Norway’s strong social security net, which includes generous parental leave policies, is a key reason the country of just over 5 million people is consistently rated one of the best places in the world to live.), [high average household income](https://www.bbc.com/worklife/article/20180709-unlike-most-millennials-norways-are-rich)(Best known for its Viking history, snow sports and jaw-dropping fjords, Norway is making a new name for itself as the only major economy in Europe where young people are getting markedly richer.\n\nPeople in their early thirties in Norway have an average annual disposable household income of around 460,000 kroner \(around $56,200\).\n\nYoung Norwegians have enjoyed a 13% rise in disposable household income in real terms compared to Generation X \(those born between 1966 and 1980\) when they were the same age. These startling figures come from the largest comparative wealth data set in the world, the Luxembourg Income Database, and were analysed in a recent report on generational incomes for the UK Think Tank The Resolution Foundation.), or even the [weather](https://www.encyclopedia.com/articles/countries-with-the-lowest-crime-rate-in-the-world/)(Norway is an extremely cold country that has long winters, so this is one of the reasons for this country’s lower crime rate. Statistics reveal that Norway typically has fewer than 30 murders per year. This country also has strict gun laws, including the types of weapons that are used for hunting and sports. Law officials also make sure that anyone who has a weapon keeps it in a secure area.).
1.1.1.4.4.4. Con: Norway has a reputation as a safe country, which has paradoxically resulted in [increased human trafficking](https://ocindex.net/country/norway)(Norway is primarily a destination country in the human trafficking trade, with victims arriving mainly via Denmark and Sweden. The number of human trafficking victims in Norway has been steadily increasing since 2007. Reports suggest that perpetrators are usually either Norwegian or share the nationality of the victims. \n\n\[...\]\n\nNorway is a destination and transit country within the human smuggling trade. It is primarily a destination for human smuggling due to its image and reputation internationally as a place of safety and public goods.).
1.1.1.5. Con: Rehabilitation is not consistently effective in [many jurisdictions](https://www.prisonlegalnews.org/news/2020/jan/7/california-prison-rehabilitation-programs-costly-and-ineffective/)(A report by the California State Auditor, released on January 31, 2019, found that rehabilitative programs currently offered by the California Department of Corrections and Rehabilitation \(CDCR\) did not assist in reducing the state’s 50 percent recidivism rate.\n\nThe report, requested by the Joint Legislative Audit Committee, evaluated the cost effectiveness of the CDCR’s budget increase of hundreds of millions of dollars since 2011, when criminal justice reforms were enacted to reduce the state’s prison population. The report highlighted problems in enrollment, staffing, curriculum and oversight, and recommended certain corrective actions.) within the US.
1.1.1.5.1. Con: Efficiency improves with time and practice. Future generations will benefit from implementing rehabilitation strategies now.
1.1.1.5.2. Pro: Many prison officials believe that rehabilitation is not effective because prisoners are [largely unresponsive](https://www.theguardian.com/society/2019/oct/29/prisoner-rehabilitation-does-not-work-says-former-prisons-boss)(Sir Martin Narey will say in a speech on Tuesday that research to establish a causal link between rehabilitation and reduced reoffending is lacking and short courses cannot fix problems caused by difficult childhoods.\n\n“The things we did to prisoners, the courses we put them on, the involvement of charities, made little or no difference,” he will tell the International Corrections and Prisons Association conference in Buenos Aires.\n\n\[...\]\n\nHe will add: “Stop fretting about rehabilitation. Politely discourage those who will urge you to believe that they have a six-week to six-month course which can undo the damage of a lifetime. The next time someone tells you they have a quick scheme which can transform lives – transform is the word of which you should be particularly suspicious – politely explain that life isn’t that simple.”) to attempted rehabilitation programs.
1.1.1.5.3. Con: Rehabilitation is most effective when planned and coordinated over time, before, during, and after release, instead of a one-off program \([p. 113](https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/pdf/10.1177/1748895818800743)(In turn, rehabilitative interventions implemented in prison are more likely to be effective if they are followed up after release, and if transition from custody to community is planned and coordinated from an early stage in the sentence. Albeit differently named, designed and delivered by different organizations, and with a focus on achieving different aims, there has been a long history of working with prisoners to address the practical problems they face upon leaving custody. While promoted by British probation services since the 1960s, the delivery of practical assistance for adult prisoners \(except lifers\) on release has been sporadic, not compulsory, generally offered by charitable and voluntary agencies \(latterly by the Probation Service\) and of variable quality.)\).
1.1.2. Pro: Society is at less risk from incarcerated prisoners being rehabilitated than inmates being punished.
1.1.2.1. Pro: Prisons that focus on rehabilitation seem to have fewer escape attempts.
1.1.2.1.1. Pro: The Iwahig Prison in the Phillippines emphasizes keeping a connection between the inmates and their local community; prisoners rarely try to escape and even [pay for some of their upkeep](https://www.aljazeera.com/features/2015/10/7/life-inside-the-philippines-prison-without-walls)(Aside from the maximum-security inmates, who are kept isolated, all convicts learn a trade, including farming, fishing, forestry and carpentry. \n\nThese income-generating activities allow the authorities to partially recoup the money spent on the prisoners’ upkeep.\n\n\[...\]\n\nAlthough penal farms see fewer escape attempts than other Philippine jails – only 20 have occurred at Iwahig in the past decade – a successful June 2014 jailbreak of seven prisoners led authorities to forbid convicts from sleeping outside the barracks.).
1.1.2.1.2. Con: The majority of prison escapees are inmates who walk away from [community correction facilities](https://slate.com/news-and-politics/2001/02/how-often-do-prisoners-escape.html)(True, there are still thousands of escapees a year. Why aren’t you hearing about them? The vast majority of escapees are “walk-aways” from community corrections facilities that have minimal supervision. Dramatic, Hollywood-style escapes from maximum security prisons are the ones that draw media attention. \n\nLike their maximum security counterparts, the minimum security walk-aways are usually recovered. State prisons reported that more escapees and AWOL prisoners were returned than escaped every year from 1995 to 1998. The last year with more escapees than recaptured prisoners was 1994, when 14,307 prisoners escaped and 13,346 were returned.\n\nFederal prison breakouts are rarer than state prison escapes. One federal prisoner escaped and was recaptured in 1999, out of a prison population of more than 115,000. He was the only one to escape in the past four years.) with minimal supervision. There are far fewer escapes from maximum security prisons.
1.1.2.2. Con: A violent criminal, while in prison, is a far lesser threat than a poorly-rehabilitated one wandering freely in society.
1.1.2.2.1. Pro: Rehabilitation programs usually result in criminals being released early \([p. 97](https://link.springer.com/content/pdf/10.1007/s40865-020-00139-3.pdf)(If need-specific treatment programs are, however, not indicated by risk assessment scores, prisoners can still enter the Prevention of Recidivism Program and are eligible for phased re-entry and early release without having to complete specific treatment modules \(i.e., a standard program\).)\). This reduces the amount of time that society is protected from the criminal.
1.1.2.2.2. Con: A poorly-rehabilitated violent criminal would not be released from prison as they would not have been rehabilitated yet.
1.1.2.3. Pro: If criminals were required to provide a predetermined number of hours of [community service](https://www.nolo.com/legal-encyclopedia/what-kind-community-service-can-judge-order.html)(In general, judges have broad discretion to decide whether and what kind of community service to order for an offender. As long as the sentence reasonably protects the public's interests and isn't unduly harsh, all different kinds of community service options are available. However, there are a few guidelines that judges must follow when crafting community service sentences.) instead of prison time, they would actually benefit society.
1.1.3. Con: Rehabilitation might not help those convicted of very violent crimes, who may be incapable of reform.
1.1.3.1. Pro: [Supermax prisons in the US](https://www.nationalreview.com/2021/08/the-problem-with-supermax-prisons/)(Super-maximum-security prisons are well-known for being the toughest prisons in America. Intended to be reserved for “the worst of the worst” offenders, they are supposed to be the last stop for prisoners beyond rehabilitation. They occupy something of a presence in our modern society; Arnold Schwarzenegger teamed up with Sylvester Stallone in a movie about two hardened criminals escaping from a supermax cell. Unfortunately, this romanticized version of grizzled criminals going away for life in a supermax prison is inaccurate and contributes to an unhealthy prison culture.), designed to house the most dangerous criminals, [do not offer rehabilitation programs](https://www.aljazeera.com/opinions/2011/8/5/supermax-prisons-21st-century-asylums)(There is no pretence of rehabilitation in supermax prisons; the purpose is harsh punishment. Prisoners endure supersized portions of psychological punishment as a result of strict and prolonged solitary confinement. Inmates are confined for 23 to 24 hours a day, every day, in cells that measure 7-by-12 square feet. It is psychological torture.), suggesting that these prisoners cannot be rehabilitated.
1.1.3.1.1. Con: Though supermax prison administrators do not attempt to rehabilitate prisoners, that does not mean that they cannot be rehabilitated.
1.1.3.1.2. Con: Supermax prisons have been criticized as [inhumane](https://www.amnesty.org.uk/press-releases/usa-federal-supermax-prison-colorado-inhuman-and-breach-international-law)(The US government’s callous and dehumanising practice of holding prisoners in prolonged solitary confinement in the country’s only federal super-maximum security prison amounts to cruel, inhuman or degrading treatment or punishment and is in violation of international law, Amnesty International said in a new report today \(16 July\).\n \nThe 50-page report - Entombed: Isolation in the US Federal Prison - reveals the severity of conditions faced by prisoners at the United States Penitentiary, Administrative Maximum facility near the city of Florence in the state of Colorado \(known as ADX Florence\).). Their practices should not be used as a yardstick for effective prison policy.
1.1.3.1.3. Pro: Many prisoners in supermax prisons are convicted terrorists or career criminals whose release would be a [public security risk](https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-17663629)(The facility, 108 miles \(173km\) from Denver, holds convicted terrorists, militant anti-government extremists, gangsters, and other criminals considered so violent and dangerous mere maximum-security prison is not safe enough.\n\n"We have only the most violent, disruptive and escape-prone inmates in the federal system," says Mark Collins, a spokesman for the prison.); their indefinite detainment is appropriate.
1.1.3.2. Pro: Attempts to rehabilitate or reintegrate prisoners convicted of violent crimes could be traumatizing for the victims and their families.
1.1.3.2.1. Con: Some rehabilitation programs, such as restorative justice, can help combat post-traumatic stress disorder in crime victims \([p. 77](https://link.springer.com/content/pdf/10.1007/s12207-019-09363-9.pdf)(Crime victimisation is a significant life event that can lead to the development of post-traumatic symptomology. Compared with the general population, victims of crime are significantly more likely to present with symptoms of post-traumatic stress disorder \(PTSD\).\n\n\[...\]\n\nThis interpersonal transgression creates an obligation for the offender to repair the damage done by such an act and restore the stakeholders to their prior status \(Zehr 1990\). During the process of repairing the harm, victims are invited to meet with the perpetrator where they are able to discuss the incident. Due to the processes unique to RJ, research has suggested that the intervention has beneficial properties that are absent in other forms of justice, including positive psychological outcomes for victims. As such, RJ has been promoted as an evidence-based intervention that has additional benefits for victims compared with customary adversarial justice.)\).
1.1.3.2.2. Pro: Violent, brutal murderers have been granted early release from prison, [causing anguish](https://www.theguardian.com/uk-news/2021/jul/13/killer-colin-pitchfork-to-be-released-from-prison-after-challenge-fails)(In early June the Parole Board ruled that Pitchfork could be released on licence, saying his behaviour in custody had been “positive and had included extensive efforts to help others”.\n\n\[...\]\n\nThe families of the victims have criticised the board’s decision. “There are some crimes so horrendous that a reprieve is not appropriate. It is an affront to natural justice,” Dawn’s uncle, Philip Musson, previously said. “He took their lives in a way which is an absolute torment to those who cared and loved these girls.”\n\n\[...\]\n\nHe pleaded guilty to two counts of murder, two of rape, two of indecent assault and one of conspiracy to pervert the course of justice. The lord chief justice at the time, Lord Lane, said: “From the point of view of the safety of the public, I doubt if he should ever be released.”) to the surviving family of their victims.
1.1.3.3. Con: Some experts believe [all people are capable of change](https://www.npr.org/sections/health-shots/2016/06/30/484053435/personality-can-change-over-a-lifetime-and-usually-for-the-better?t=1650016054068)(And while personality traits are relatively stable over time, they can and often do gradually change across the life span. What's more, those changes are usually for the better. Many studies, including some of my own, show that most adults become more agreeable, conscientious and emotionally resilient as they age. But these changes tend to unfold across years or decades, rather than days or weeks. Sudden, dramatic changes in personality are rare.). Regardless of whether they will ever be released, efforts should be made to reform even the most serious criminals.
1.1.3.4. Con: Basing prison systems on the impact they will have on high-risk prisoners, given that [most inmates are low-risk](https://www.prisonpolicy.org/reports/pie2022.html#lowlevel)(Most justice-involved people in the U.S. are not accused of serious crimes; more often, they are charged with misdemeanors or non-criminal violations. Yet even low-level offenses, like technical violations of probation and parole, can lead to incarceration and other serious consequences. Rather than investing in community-driven safety initiatives, cities and counties are still pouring vast amounts of public resources into the processing and punishment of these minor offenses.), is not reasonable.
1.1.3.5. Pro: Some experts consider psychopathy to be [uncurable](https://www.psychologytoday.com/blog/wicked-deeds/201408/psychopathic-criminals-cannot-be-cured)(According to mental health experts, the short answer to this question is no. Dr. Nigel Blackwood, a leading Forensic Psychiatrist at King’s College London, has stated that adult psychopaths can be treated or managed but not cured.\n\nBlackwood explains that psychopaths do not fear the pain of punishment and they are not bothered by social stigmatization. Psychopaths are indifferent to the expectations of society and reject its condemnation of their criminal behavior. According to Blackwood and others, callous and unemotional psychopaths simply do not respond to punishment the way that normal people do. Consequently, adult psychopaths in prison are much harder to reform or rehabilitate than other criminals with milder or no antisocial personality disorders.).
1.1.3.5.1. Con: Evidence exists that appropriate treatment programs may have some effectiveness in treating psychopathy \([pp. 16-17](https://www.researchgate.net/profile/Anthony-Beech/publication/6999230_Psychopathy_Theory_Measurement_and_Treatment/links/0f317536182f364564000000/Psychopathy-Theory-Measurement-and-Treatment.pdf?origin=publication_detail)(It would seem that the pessimistic view of treatment effectiveness on psychopaths has been fuelled by the unsuccessful outcomes of treatment programs inappropriately used. It appears that when an appropriately designed treatment program is implemented, effectiveness of treatment can be demonstrated.)\).
1.1.3.5.2. Con: Psychopathy [may not exist](https://www.psychologytoday.com/gb/blog/wiring-the-mind/201408/psychopaths-the-worst-people-who-don-t-exist)(Despite being in common usage for years, the psychiatrist’s bible, the Diagnostic and Statistical Manual does not include psychopathy as a diagnosis. The World Health Organization, the American Psychiatric Association and countless other professional bodies have also chosen to avoid the term. Instead of the very real risk of branding people suffering from an often tormenting mental health condition with an emotionally loaded stigmata, they tend to use diagnoses like ‘antisocial personality disorder’. Far from being a useful description, ‘psychopath’ conjures up the perfect image of someone you wouldn’t be desperate to help, someone as inhuman as those previously branded evil.) as a condition. If that is the case, whether it can be treated or not is irrelevant to discussions about rehabilitating criminals.
1.1.3.5.3. Pro: Psychopathy is a [personality disorder](https://www.businessinsider.com/psychopaths-cannot-be-cured-heres-why-2018-2?r=US&IR=T)(Like many personality traits, psychopathy is a spectrum. About 1-2% of men and 0.3-0.7% of women in the general population are estimated to be true psychopaths, but for the rest of us, we fall on the scale somewhere lower down.\n\nPeople who experience psychopathic traits, such as ruthlessness, charisma, impulsivity, and persuasiveness, tend to get through life just fine. Even full-blown psychopaths can be very successful — they just won't ever be the same as everyone else.\n\n\[...\]\n\nIt also means a true psychopath can never be "cured."\n\n"From what I've read, what I've heard, what I've seen and experienced so far, people with dark triad personality disorders cannot and will not change," said Perpetua Neo, a doctor of psychology and therapist, in an earlier article for Business Insider.), not a mental illness. This makes it uncurable.
1.1.4. Con: If society feels that justice is not being done, it could result in vigilantism \([p. 224](https://www.researchgate.net/profile/Jan-De-Keijser/publication/263492175_Public_support_for_vigilantism_confidence_in_police_and_police_responsiveness/links/56a9fc5208aef6e05df426a7/Public-support-for-vigilantism-confidence-in-police-and-police-responsiveness.pdf?origin=publication_detail)(This article provides an empirical test of the common assumption that public support for vigilantism is affected by confidence in police. Aside from assessing the role of diffuse \(general\) confidence in police, we also tested whether police response on a situational level affects how the public views an act of vigilantism. Respondents \(N=385\) were presented with a vignette about vigilantism. Using an experimental between-subjects design, we varied police responsiveness \(high/low\) to precipitating crime as well as vigilante violence \(high/low\). Diffused confidence in police was a significant predictor of support for vigilantism. Additionally, both experimental factors played an important role: low police responsiveness and low vigilante violence led to more support for vigilantism. Citizens are thus sensitive to situational variation when judging a crime. Our findings also emphasise the importance of police action on a local level for the formation of public opinion)\).
1.1.4.1. Con: Even though there are [many cases](http://thehill.com/blogs/pundits-blog/finance/309544-why-have-no-ceos-been-punished-for-the-financial-crisis)(Nearly a decade after the fraud and irresponsible actions at Wall Street banks, Countrywide Financial, and other companies brought the nation to the brink of total economic collapse, there have been no prosecutions against their key executives.\n\nIs our justice system incapable of pressing these cases to their rightful conclusion? Or just unwilling? None of the chief executives at the companies that caused the 2008 global financial crisis has been held fully accountable. Nobody from Lehman Brothers or Citigroup has been criminally charged. No top executives at Bear Stearns have been indicted.) where high-profile criminal actions result in no prosecutions, people rarely resort to vigilantism.
1.1.4.2. Pro: Members of the public taking justice into their own hands could seriously erode the [state's monopoly on violence](https://www.britannica.com/topic/state-monopoly-on-violence)(state monopoly on violence, in political science and sociology, the concept that the state alone has the right to use or authorize the use of physical force. It is widely regarded as a defining characteristic of the modern state.\n\nIn his lecture “Politics as a Vocation” \(1918\), the German sociologist Max Weber defines the state as a “human community that \(successfully\) claims the monopoly of the legitimate use of physical force within a given territory.” Under feudalism, no lords, including the king, could claim a monopoly over the use of violence, since their vassals promised to serve them but remained free to exercise power in their fiefdoms. Moreover, the king and the landed nobility had to share power or compete with the Roman Catholic Church. The modern state, according to Weber, emerged by expropriating the means of political organization and domination, including violence, and by establishing the legitimacy of its rule.).
1.1.4.2.1. Con: The state's monopoly on violence is dependent on the state's continued public support. Therefore, true authority over violence lies with the public \([pp. 1-2](https://www.hks.harvard.edu/sites/default/files/centers/cid/files/publications/faculty-working-papers/071.pdf)(Order, we argue, is problematic. Citizens can and do revolt, employing their control over private means of violence to overturn the state. We also note that states that have been founded consensually may, ex post, become predatory; and that a predatory band may form a state that, ex post, enhances the collective welfare. The existence and properties of a state thus can not be assumed. In search of an approach that treats both as endogenous, we instead turn to rational choice analysis.)\).
1.1.4.2.2. Pro: If emotionally distressed victims were allowed to take revenge on the offender, they might overcompensate for their perceived injustice \([p. 482](https://wordpress.nmsu.edu/ketelaar/files/2020/06/Van-den-Bos-2003-On-the-Subjective-Quality-of-Social-Justice.pdf)(This article argues that it is not uncommon for people forming justice judgments to lack information that is most relevant in the particular situation. In information-uncertain conditions, people may therefore construct justice judgments by relying on how they feel about the events they have encountered, and justice judgments may hence be strongly influenced by affect information. Findings show that in information-uncertain conditions, the affective states that people had been in prior and unrelated to the justice event indeed strongly influenced their justice judgments. These findings thus reveal that in situations of information uncertainty, people’s judgments of justice can be very subjective, susceptible to affective states that have no logical relationship with the justice judgments they are constructing. Implications for the social psychology of justice and the literature on social cognition and affect are discussed.)\). Vigilantism must therefore be avoided by ensuring that the offender is punished sufficiently by the state.
1.1.4.2.3. Pro: Hobbes argued that the state’s monopoly on violence is the most important factor in preventing chaos in society \([p. 2](https://library.fes.de/pdf-files/iez/12036.pdf)(In Leviathan \(1651\), Thomas Hobbes argued for a strong state to overcome a »state of nature« in which life is solitary, poor, nasty, brutish, and short. A strong state was necessary to impose order, he reasoned, because human beings left to their own devices would engage in constant conflict in their search for security. So for Hobbes, any state able to monopolize the use of force was better than none.)\). Any erosion of this monopoly could have disastrous consequences.
1.1.4.2.3.1. Pro: The state's monopoly on violence should be maintained as vigilantes often take revenge upon [innocent people](https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-england-tyne-54157912)(A man suffered face and head injuries in what police have called a case of mistaken identity by vigilantes.\n\nThe 38-year-old man was assaulted on Woodhorn Lane, Ashington, at about 18:30 BST on Saturday.\n\nNorthumbria Police said it is believed he was attacked after being mistaken for a man accused of sharing indecent posts on social media.).
1.1.4.2.4. Con: If society feels that prisons do more harm than good and that would-be prisoners deserve protection and rehabilitation, it could result in obstruction of justice and fighting police. This could also erode the state's monopoly on violence.
1.1.5. Con: Rehabilitation is arbitrary, difficult to control, and challenging to measure.
1.1.5.1. Pro: Focusing solely on rehabilitation could lead to excessive and arbitrarily long treatments.
1.1.5.1.1. Pro: The notion of proportionality ensures that prisoners in a retributive system are not held for an inappropriate amount of time \([p. 704](https://ir.lawnet.fordham.edu/cgi/viewcontent.cgi?referer=https://www.google.com/&httpsredir=1&article=1440&context=faculty_scholarship)(These two aspects of retributivism, permissive and mandatory, have traditionally come to represent two different types of retributivism. The former has been known in the literature as “strong” or “positive” retributivism, and the latter known as “weak,” “negative,” or “limiting” retributivism. The type that is appropriate as the basis for the Eighth Amendment prohibition on excessive punishment is the “weak,” “negative,” and “limiting” kind. Retributivism under the Eighth Amendment is negative not only because it serves as a statement of what not to do, but also because it serves as a side constraint on the socially desirable practice of punishment.)\). Prisoners being forced to undergo rehabilitation may be held for much longer.
1.1.5.1.1.1. Con: Many countries with rehabilitative prison systems still have [caps on the length](https://www.vox.com/future-perfect/2019/2/12/18184070/maximum-prison-sentence-cap-mass-incarceration)(Other countries show this can work. European nations tend to have shorter prison sentences than the US, and certainly fewer people in prison, along with roughly equal or lower violent crime rates. Norway in particular caps the great majority of prison sentences at 21 years — and its violent crime and reoffending rates are lower than the US’s. \(The cap does have some exceptions, as I’ll explain later.\)) of prison sentences.
1.1.5.1.2. Con: Many states which focus on punishment allow their judges to hand out [life sentences](https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/magazine-22912075)(Life sentences that truly mean a lifetime in prison are rare in the UK but common in the US. Why is this punishment so prevalent in the US?\n\nLast week, an English court handed a whole-life sentence to Dale Cregan for murdering four people, including two policewomen.\n\nThat penalty means he will never be eligible for release, and it puts him in rare company, making him one of about 50 people in the UK serving such a sentence.\n\nHad he been in the US, he would have been less of an anomaly.\n\nIn the US, at least 40,000 people are imprisoned without hope for parole, including 2,500 under the age of 18.). Punishment-based systems, therefore, can lead to excessive and arbitrarily prolonged penalties.
1.1.5.1.2.1. Pro: Some methods of imprisonment can result in inappropriately long sentences. For example, [debtors' prisons still exist](https://www.themarshallproject.org/2015/02/24/debtors-prisons-then-and-now-faq)(In the United States, debtors’ prisons were banned under federal law in 1833. A century and a half later, in 1983, the Supreme Court affirmed that incarcerating indigent debtors was unconstitutional under the Fourteenth Amendment’s Equal Protection clause. Yet, citizens like Sanders and Ford are, to this day, routinely jailed after failing to repay debt. Though de jure debtors’ prisons are a thing of the past, de facto debtors’ imprisonment is not. So what do we really know about modern-day debtors’ imprisonment – how it returned, when, and where?) despite being outlawed by governments around the world.
1.1.5.1.3. Con: Timed prison terms wouldn't need to exist in a system that focuses on rehabilitation, where rehabilitation staff could determine a prisoner's fitness to leave the care facility and return to society.
1.1.5.1.3.1. Pro: A portion of the prisoner's sentence could include mandatory [educational or skill targets](https://www.americanprogress.org/article/education-opportunities-prison-key-reducing-crime/)(There is a logical argument for prison education: It is a cost-effective way to reduce crime and leads to long-term benefits across the entire U.S. population. In 2016, the RAND Corporation produced a report that showed that individuals who participate in any type of educational program while in prison are 43 percent less likely to return to prison. In addition to reducing recidivism, education can improve outcomes from one generation to the next. Research shows that children with parents with college degrees are more likely to complete college, which can create social mobility for families.); accomplishing the minimum requirements would reduce their sentence.
1.1.5.2. Con: Many countries have [focused on helping rehabilitate prisoners](https://www.kriminalvarden.se/swedish-prison-and-probation-service/rehabilitation/)(All occupational activities in prison aim to improve inmates' chances of leading a drug free and law abiding life after having served their sentence. \n\nIn prison it is compulsory to partake in occupational activites for six hours daily, Monday through Friday. Activites can be working with assembling subcontracted products, indoor maintenance or laundry, for example.\n\nPrisoners can also study part-time or full-time. The Prison and Probation Service has our own school system with over a 120 accredited high school professors. This enables prisoners to continue their education even if they are translocated to another prison facility.\n\nActivities also include attending rehabilitation programs that target drug- and alcohol abuse, violent behaviour, aggression and criminal behaviour. There are group sessions as well as indivual sessions. Usually programs are combined with work or study.) by creating strict guidelines, best practices, and optimal observational and therapeutic analysis strategies.
1.1.5.3. Con: Rehabilitation programs such as the [principles of effective intervention](http://law.jrank.org/pages/1936/Rehabilitation-Does-correctional-rehabilitation-work.html)(Based on meta-analyses of treatment studies, they found that in rehabilitation programs that conformed to the principles of effective intervention, recidivism was about 25 percentage points lower in the treatment as opposed to the control group \(Andrews and Bonta; Cullen and Gendreau\). These principles include: \(1\) target the known predictors of recidivism for change; \(2\) use cognitive-behavioral treatments that reinforce prosocial attitudes and behavior, seek to challenge and extinguish criminal thinking patterns, and provide alternative, prosocial ways of acting; \(3\) focus treatment interventions on high-risk offenders; \(4\) try to take into account characteristics of offenders \(e.g., I.Q.\) that might affect their responsivity to treatment; \(5\) employ staff that are well trained and interpersonally sensitive; and \(6\) provide offenders with aftercare once they leave the program \(Gendreau\).) have provided concrete statistical evidence of their efficacy.
1.1.5.4. Pro: Rehabilitation is harder to measure objectively than the length of a prison term.
1.1.5.4.1. Pro: Rehabilitation can be faked.
1.1.5.4.1.1. Pro: Some people have been told to initially [fake their rehabilitation](https://www.uk-rehab.com/treatment-rehab/addiction-recovery/why-are-people-told-to-fake-it-to-make-it-in-recovery/)(This is better understood by thinking about the universal law that states that if you do good things, good things are likely to happen to you. Hopefully, your experiences with addiction have shown you what happens when you do the wrong things, so faking it to make it could be seen as a way of creating good karma for yourself.\n\nFaking it to make it is something all humans do to at least some extent. It is unlikely that you would survive very long in the modern world if you acted on every thought in your head. Sometimes you just need to act like the person you want to be rather than the person you really feel like at that moment. So the advice to ‘fake it to make it’ is not telling you to do anything new – it is really about doing something you already do but more effectively.) in order to help manifest it into a reality.
1.1.5.4.1.1.1. Con: Faking rehabilitation is an [unreliable short-term strategy](https://www.uk-rehab.com/treatment-rehab/addiction-recovery/why-are-people-told-to-fake-it-to-make-it-in-recovery/)(Faking it to make it is only a short-term strategy. By initially acting as if you are going to succeed, you start to experience some success in life. As your confidence grows in your ability to shape the future, there is no longer any need for you to fake it – it is just something you do in order to get the ball rolling.) for some and is not a long-term solution for anyone.
1.1.5.4.1.2. Con: [Faking an addiction](https://www.clearviewtreatment.com/resources/blog/factitious-disorder/)(You may have heard of Munchausen syndrome, a disorder in which a person will lie about having a disease or medical condition in order to get treatment. This is also known as Factitious Disorder.\n\nAccording to a researcher at the Department of Criminology and Criminal Justice at Central Connecticut State University, it has been documented that patients have checked themselves in for drug and alcohol rehab when in fact they do not really have a drug or alcohol problem.) is easier than faking rehabilitation.
1.1.5.4.1.3. Con: Someone who is able to convincingly fake something about themselves likely has an [unresolved mental issue](https://www.clearviewtreatment.com/resources/blog/factitious-disorder/)(What the patient does need is treatment for the deep need for attention and perhaps for pseudologia fantastica, or compulsive lying. A study conducted at the University of California demonstrated that habitual liars have more white matter than gray matter in their brain, making a case for a biological reason for faking a long-term, serious illness. But otherwise, another condition, such as a personality disorder, may be serious enough to propel one into pretending to be an addict) that needs treatment; they are unlikely to be provided with such treatment in a prison.
1.1.5.4.1.3.1. Pro: Psychopathic criminals have been known to [manipulate prison staff](http://news.bbc.co.uk/1/hi/uk/7899143.stm)("The majority of serial sexual homicide offenders will be diagnosed as having psychopathic personality disorder, which the vast majority of clinicians would view as being problematic in terms of treatment," he says.\n\n"Such psychopaths are extremely good at manipulating prison staff, as well as mental health professionals, and over time they can eventually be perceived as being 'cured' and ready for reintroduction into society.) into believing they are rehabilitated.
1.1.5.4.2. Con: Recidivism rates can be used to evaluate the effectiveness of rehabilitation \([p. 8](https://www.unodc.org/documents/justice-and-prison-reform/18-02303_ebook.pdf)(Facilitating offender reintegration is a complex task, and the impact of specific interventions is often difficult to measure. Reduced criminal recidivism remains the ultimate indicator of successful social reintegration programmes. “Recidivism” \(“reoffending”\) refers to whether a person who is the object of a criminal justice intervention reoffends later on.)\).
1.1.5.4.2.1. Con: An offender could take months or years to reoffend; this puts society at risk while the effectiveness of rehabilitation is being evaluated.
1.1.5.4.2.1.1. Con: Every moment the rehabilitated criminal refrains from committing the same crime is a measurable success.
1.1.5.4.2.2. Pro: Reoffending rates are hard to measure, but that applies equally to both punishment-based systems as well as rehabilitation-based systems \([p. 4](https://assets.publishing.service.gov.uk/government/uploads/system/uploads/attachment_data/file/564634/proven-reoffending-definitions-measurement-Oct16.pdf)(Measuring true reoffending is difficult. Official records are taken from either the police or courts, but they will underestimate the true level of reoffending because only a proportion of crime is detected and sanctioned and not all crimes and sanctions are recorded on one central system. Other methods of measuring reoffending, such as self report studies, are likely to also underestimate the rate.)\).
1.1.5.4.3. Con: Quantifiable rehabilitation goals could include the number of community service hours completed, the number of visits with a life coach or job counselor, or the number of times the inmate exhibited good behavior.
1.1.5.4.4. Pro: The ambiguity of rehabilitation makes it more difficult to measure; some people might be able to rehabilitate faster than others.
1.1.5.4.5. Pro: Prioritizing punishment ensures that everyone is treated [equally under the law](https://studentsforliberty.org/north-america/blog/dont-tread-on-anyone/equal-treatment-under-the-law/)(Equal treatment under the law is an ancient principle which is still of vital importance in the modern world. Almost every constitution worldwide recognizes this fundamental concept, in some form at least. Also known as equality before the law, or isonomy, the basic principle recognizes that all individuals should be treated in exactly the same manner by the law, while all persons should be subject to the same laws.\n\nArticle 7 of the Universal Declaration of Human Rights formalizes this basic right as: \n\nAll are equal before the law and are entitled without any discrimination to equal protection of the law. All are entitled to equal protection against any discrimination in violation of this Declaration and against any incitement to such discrimination.), a key aspect of deterrence. In punishment-based systems, criminals will be sentenced according to concrete [precedents](https://biotech.law.lsu.edu/map/TheImportanceofPrecedent.html)(In a common law system, judges are obliged to make their rulings as consistent as reasonably possible with previous judicial decisions on the same subject. The Constitution accepted most of the English common law as the starting point for American law. Situations still arise that involve rules laid down in cases decided more than 200 years ago. Each case decided by a common law court becomes a precedent, or guideline, for subsequent decisions involving similar disputes. These decisions are not binding on the legislature, which can pass laws to overrule unpopular court decisions. Unless these laws are determined to be unconstitutional by the Supreme Court, they preempt the common law precedent cases. Judges deciding cases are bound by the new law, rather than the precedent cases.) rather than abstract rehabilitation concepts.
1.1.5.4.5.1. Pro: Prison as a punishment lasts a predetermined amount of time, as established by a judge. In contrast, uncertainty over whether a criminal is genuinely rehabilitated may result in significant variations in sentence length due to the effort invested by each prisoner.
1.1.5.4.5.1.1. Con: Prisoners in punishment-based systems can still be given inconsistent or [unlimited sentences](https://www.theguardian.com/law/2020/dec/03/indefinite-sentences-the-greatest-single-stain-on-justice-system)(A former supreme court justice has called for urgent action in dealing with prisoners languishing under indefinite sentences, branding the now defunct scheme “the greatest single stain on our criminal justice system”.\n\nLord Brown, a justice of the supreme court between 2009 and 2012, said the imprisonment for public protection \(IPP\) regime, under which offenders were handed a minimum term but no maximum term, required “long overdue” reform.\n\nDespite the use of the sentencing power being scrapped in 2012, more than 3,200 prisoners remain locked up under the regime, including those who have been recalled to prison, prompting critics to brand it the “never-ending sentence”.).
1.1.5.4.5.1.1.1. Pro: The [general public was outraged](https://www.npr.org/sections/thetwo-way/2016/06/06/481010919/california-rape-case-sentence-sparks-outrage)(But others, including the Santa Clara District Attorney Jeff Rosen, say Turner, who was a top swimmer at Stanford, got off easy when he was sentenced to six months in county jail and three years' probation. Turner was convicted in March on three felony counts. Ewing's golf-club theft earned him a sentence of 25 years to life.) due to California's three strikes law.
1.1.5.4.5.1.2. Pro: An approach focusing on proportionate punishment still considers the rights of prisoners not to be unduly punished beyond a reasonable sentence \([p. 466](https://link.springer.com/content/pdf/10.1007/s11572-021-09571-y.pdf)(Consider next proportionality. This refers to matching relationship between the severity of a punishment and the seriousness of the crime: we say that a punishment is ‘proportionate’ if and only if its harshness is commensurate to the gravity of the offense. The classical account of proportionality thus understood is lex talionis, or the principle that the harm one inflicts on others should be inflicted in return. Contemporary penologists, however, hold the more sophisticated view that a punishment is proportionate if and only if its severity is commensurate to the seriousness of the crime, where seriousness is some function of the harm or wrong caused or risked and the offender’s culpability. From this account, then, a killing is more serious than assault; an intentional killing is more serious than a negligent killing; and so on.)\).
1.1.5.4.5.2. Con: Requiring people to meet the same rehabilitation standards would be treating people equally under the law.
1.1.5.4.5.3. Con: Prioritizing punishment does not ensure that people are treated equally if there is a range of possible sentences.
1.1.5.4.5.4. Con: Prisoners being rehabilitated could be held for the same amount of time as they would have been in a punitive system. The only difference would be what the prisoners do while incarcerated.
1.1.5.4.5.5. Pro: Some prisoners receive substantially reduced sentences through [parole](https://www.gov.uk/government/organisations/parole-board/about)(Our job is to determine if someone is safe to release. We do that with great care, and public safety is our number one priority. We deal with 25,000 cases a year, which are referred to us by the Ministry of Justice once prisoners have served the punishment determined by the courts. Parole Board decisions are solely focused on whether a prisoner would represent a significant risk to the public after release. The risk assessment is based on detailed evidence found in the dossier \(a collection of documents relating to the prisoner\) and evidence provided at the oral hearing.) hearings, which consider the extent to which a prisoner has been rehabilitated. This can result in two people who committed the same crime being incarcerated for different amounts of time.
1.1.5.4.5.5.1. Con: Considering factors other than the crime that was committed when sentencing does not represent treating people unequally under the law.
1.1.5.4.6. Con: Rehabilitation being hard to measure objectively is not an argument against its use, but only an acknowledgment of the difficulty of implementing it.
1.1.5.4.6.1. Con: Considering the efficacy of a policy without considering the difficulty of its implementation is not possible.
1.1.5.4.6.2. Con: The difficulty in measuring rehabilitation makes it hard to comprehend the harshness of sentences.
1.1.5.4.6.3. Pro: Many rehabilitation programs are [hard to implement](https://www.theguardian.com/society/2015/mar/11/probation-led-crash-course-harder-jail-sentence-car-crimes)(Where community rehabilitation ‘is harder than a short-term jail sentence’\n\nIn a Manchester scheme, those convicted of car crimes and given intensive community orders are brought face to face with the consequences\n\n\[...\]\n\nPugh adds: “Many offenders freely admit that the ICO is harder than a short-term jail sentence \[because\] the activities and interventions are much more victim focused.”\n\nDaniel Cherrington, an ex-offender who was convicted of a vehicle crime, testifies to that: “A lot of offenders find that it’s easier to go to prison. If you get sentenced in a magistrates court to six months then you’ll serve four months in prison, take a third off for your guilty plea, you’re out after eight weeks and you’re free. If you’re on an ICO you might not be ready for the help. A lot of people aren’t. You’ve got to want to change, and you’ve got to stop taking drugs and committing crimes. I’m only just getting through it myself.”) and challenging to measure, but this does not mean they are less effective than prison.
1.1.5.4.6.4. Pro: The difficulty in implementing prison rehabilitation programs may be due to the novelty of the idea itself. Over time, the logistics and operational procedures should be clarified, smoothing out the process for other prisons to implement these programs more effectively.
1.1.5.4.7. Pro: Leaving rehabilitation up to the discretion of prisons based on a judgement about an inmate's promise is highly subjective and could be abused in racist or political ways.
1.1.5.4.7.1. Con: Rehabilitation is not up to the discretion of the prison administration or officers. [Dedicated staff or organizations](https://www.justiceinspectorates.gov.uk/hmiprisons/our-expectations/prison-expectations/rehabilitation-and-release-planning/reducing-risk-rehabilitation-and-progression/)(- There is good cooperation and communication between various organisations and departments delivering rehabilitative work in the prison.\n\n\[...\]\n\n- Staff have a good working knowledge of the rehabilitation services available and actively promote them.\n\n- Relevant voluntary and community sector organisations are supported to work with prisoners.\n\n- A named manager is responsible for coordinating the work of voluntary and community sector organisations.\n\n- Partners are encouraged to take part in prison training, for example suicide and self-harm prevention.) are tasked with delivering rehabilitation, while a [parole board](https://www.prospectmagazine.co.uk/politics/the-parole-system-is-broken-this-is-how-to-fix-it)(Parole is how the state decides when offenders—primarily those who have either been given indeterminate sentences, or determinate sentences after being convicted of more serious crimes—should be given a second chance. The Parole Board reviews their conduct in prison and their attempts at rehabilitation, and decides if they are ready to once more become part of society’s throng.) is normally responsible for determining whether a prisoner is rehabilitated or not.
1.1.6. Con: Some prisoners, especially those who are high-risk or violent, may not want to be rehabilitated.
1.1.6.1. Pro: Rehabilitation assumes a level of motivation on the part of the prisoner. Sufficient punitive action could reform a prisoner out of their offending behavior without the prisoner needing to be motivated to improve themselves.
1.1.6.2. Con: Punishment can make a person receptive to rehabilitation where they might not otherwise be so.

1.1.6.2.2. Pro: Some people claim that being sent to jail was the [best thing](https://inews.co.uk/inews-lifestyle/women/prison-best-thing-happen-to-me-destroyed-women-inside-1188621)(If you had peered through my window when I was growing up with a loving family in Reading, you wouldn’t have guessed I would end up in prison. 1.1.6.3. Why do some jail inmates not access available programs and services? Drawn from a longitudinal study, 261 adults were assessed shortly upon incarceration and re-assessed prior to transfer or release from a county jail. Of the participants in need of treatment, 18.5% did not participate in any formal treatment programs or religious programs and services. Untreated inmates were disproportionately young and male and less likely to report pre-incarceration cocaine dependence. Treatment participation varied little as a function of race or symptoms of mental illness. The most common reason for non-participation was the belief that one would not be around long enough to participate in programs. 1.1.6.4.
