1.4.1.1.2. Con: In most prisons, contact with family members is a [right, not a privilege](https://www.apt.ch/en/knowledge-hub/detention-focus-database/contacts-outside-world/family-visits)(Family visits are a right and not a privilege, and upholding the right to family is more than just about allowing the act of visits to occur. There are many elements that must be considered – for example, material conditions for visits, and consideration for detainees with particular needs or in a situation of vulnerability.), allowing prisoners to maintain their familial networks intact.
1.4.1.1.2.1. Pro: Being allowed to [remain in contact](https://www.prisonpolicy.org/blog/2021/12/21/family_contact/)(The research is clear: visitation, mail, phone, and other forms of contact between incarcerated people and their families have positive impacts for everyone — including better health, reduced recidivism, and improvement in school. Here’s a roundup of over 50 years of empirical study, and a reminder that prisons and jails often pay little more than lip service to the benefits of family contact.) with family members is vital for simultaneously helping inmates behave themselves while in prison and giving them something to look forward to when they get released.
1.4.1.1.2.2. Pro: -> See 1.1.9.1.5.4.
1.4.1.1.2.3. Con: If a prisoner's [family members](https://nypost.com/2018/10/06/this-loving-family-raised-four-generations-of-criminals/)(Bobby Bogle wasn’t the first to break the law in his family, and he’d by no means be the last. It was a crime spree that spanned four generations and crossed multiple states from Texas to Oregon, beginning in the early 1920s — with ancestors who were moonshiners and carnival workers — and involving hundreds of felonies covering just about every genre of lawlessness, from murder and sodomy to burglary and insurance fraud.) are also criminals, allowing them to maintain contact could be counterproductive.
1.4.1.1.3. Pro: Removing humans from their social context is [psychologically damaging](https://aspe.hhs.gov/reports/psychological-impact-incarceration-implications-post-prison-adjustment-0)(Increased sentence length and a greatly expanded scope of incarceration resulted in prisoners experiencing the psychological strains of imprisonment for longer periods of time, many persons being caught in the web of incarceration who ordinarily would not have been \(e.g., drug offenders\), and the social costs of incarceration becoming increasingly concentrated in minority communities \(because of differential enforcement and sentencing policies\).\n\nThus, in the first decade of the 21st century, more people have been subjected to the pains of imprisonment, for longer periods of time, under conditions that threaten greater psychological distress and potential long-term dysfunction, and they will be returned to communities that have already been disadvantaged by a lack of social services and resources.) because humans are heavily reliant on their social groups.
1.4.1.1.3.1. Pro: Prisoners who deliberately isolate themselves within prison settings have been [diagnosed with symptoms](https://aspe.hhs.gov/reports/psychological-impact-incarceration-implications-post-prison-adjustment-0)(The self-imposed social withdrawal and isolation may mean that they retreat deeply into themselves, trust virtually no one, and adjust to prison stress by leading isolated lives of quiet desperation. In extreme cases, especially when combined with prisoner apathy and loss of the capacity to initiate behavior on one's own, the pattern closely resembles that of clinical depression. Long-term prisoners are particularly vulnerable to this form of psychological adaptation.) similar to clinical depression.
1.4.1.1.3.2. Pro: Harsh prison conditions alienate criminals from their community and foster poor relationships with authority figures, making it more difficult for them to reintegrate into society upon release.
1.4.1.1.3.2.1. Con: Inmates likely already had difficulty respecting authority and were likely alienated from their communities even before going to prison.
1.4.1.1.3.2.2. Pro: Harsh punishment could persuade criminals to learn how to get away with crime, or encourage dishonest behavior without deterring crime in an effective manner.
1.4.1.1.3.3. Pro: Practices such as solitary confinement [exacerbate mental health issues](https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2020/jul/31/british-prisons-are-inhumane-and-do-not-prevent-most-of-them-should-go)(For the past four months, two-thirds of Britain’s prisoners have been in quasi-solitary confinement, locked in cells for at least 23 hours a day. According to the Prison Reform Trust, evidence indicates this does permanent mental health damage. At this point, prison becomes a life sentence. On any basis, it is barbaric.) among prisoners \([p. 2126](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC5105008/pdf/AJPH.2016.303468.pdf)(In the decade since meeting Mr. Woodfox, I have spoken with, examined, and consulted on dozens of prisoners in solitary confinement, many of them older, nearly all chronically ill. During that time, a growing scientific literature has established the catastrophic psychological impact that solitary confinement can have on juveniles and the mentally ill. In 2012, the American Psychiatric Association recommended against solitary confinement for the mentally ill; in a 2016 Washington Post article, President Obama called for a ban on solitary for juveniles; and in 2016, the US National Commission on Correctional Healthcare decried solitary confinement extending beyond 15 days as cruel, inhumane, and harmful.)\).
1.4.1.1.3.3.1. Pro: The UN has judged uninterrupted solitary confinement to be a [form of torture](https://theconversation.com/solitary-confinement-by-any-other-name-is-still-torture-149670)(The United Nation’s Mandela rules prohibit solitary confinement of prisoners for 22 hours or more a day without meaningful human contact, and prolonged solitary confinement of more than 15 consecutive days. Breaking these parameters is defined as torture.).
1.4.1.1.3.3.1.1. Pro: Torture can cause [PTSD](https://www.nhs.uk/mental-health/conditions/post-traumatic-stress-disorder-ptsd/overview/)(Post-traumatic stress disorder \(PTSD\) is an anxiety disorder caused by very stressful, frightening or distressing events.\n\[...\]\nSomeone with PTSD often relives the traumatic event through nightmares and flashbacks, and may experience feelings of isolation, irritability and guilt.\n\nThey may also have problems sleeping, such as insomnia, and find concentrating difficult.\n\nThese symptoms are often severe and persistent enough to have a significant impact on the person's day-to-day life.), a condition which will make it [more difficult](https://www.disability-benefits-help.org/working-ability/post-traumatic-stress-disorder)(The ability to continue working with post traumatic stress disorder, or PTSD, is often dependent on the severity, frequency and duration of the symptoms you experience, including how common and pronounced your episodes or flashbacks of the triggering event are.\n\nPTSD is an anxiety disorder that results from you living through or witnessing a traumatic event in which your life or the lives of others were threatened. The exact causes of the condition are unknown, and the reason some people who experience trauma develop PTSD while others do not is not known either. Regardless, PTSD changes the way the body reacts to stress, including fundamental changes in the chemicals and hormones that communicate information in the nervous system.\n\nNot only can the symptoms of PTSD affect your ability to work, but the treatments required for managing the ailment can as well, including necessary therapy and medications.) for tortured prisoners to find employment following their release \([p. 101](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC4590125/pdf/10.1177_2049463713483596.pdf)(Many refugees in the developed world are survivors of torture and present with health needs without their traumatic experience being disclosed or identified. Chronic pain is a common problem, as are symptoms of post-traumatic stress disorder \(PTSD\), anxiety, depression, and other distress.)\).
1.4.1.1.3.3.2. Con: If a prisoner is [harming other prisoners or staff](https://www.nj.com/politics/2016/12/christie_vetoes_bill_limiting_solitary_confinement.html)(In his veto message, the governor said the use of administrative segregation, a form of isolation, in New Jersey's prisons "is carefully considered, and where appropriate, is guided by medical and mental health professionals considering the individual health conditions of an inmate while balancing the need to protect other inmates and staff."), putting that prisoner in solitary confinement could be a way to prevent the greater harm of allowing them to remain in the general prison population.
1.4.1.1.3.4. Con: Prisons can operate as a society inside another society where inmates have small social groups, which can create [deeper bonds](https://courses.lumenlearning.com/boundless-sociology/chapter/group-dynamics/)(Since it is easier for fewer people to agree on goals and to coordinate their work, smaller groups are often more cohesive than larger groups. Group cohesiveness may suffer, though, if the group lacks enough members to perform its tasks well.\n\nAn intimate community is one in which some members recognize and are recognized by all of the others, and most of the members recognize and are recognized by many of the others. This is in contrast to \(usually larger\) communities where members are known and interact mostly within their own subgroup, such as a neighborhood, department, or occupation. The contrast between the two types is illustrated by comparing hamlet with town, military company with battalion, parish church with diocese, or a country school with a huge urban one.) between members and help protect individuals from psychological damage \([p. 1047](https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/pdf/10.1080/07418825.2019.1700298?needAccess=true)(Exponential Random Graph Models revealed that network structure in prison resembles known friendship network structure outside prison, including reciprocity and transitivity in social ties \(“the friends of my friends are my friends”\) and homophily \(i.e., a preference for similar others\) on major sociodemographic dimensions such as religion and age. In conclusion, this study shows that a social network approach leads to valuable insights in social organization in prison that are also relevant for prison policy.)\).
1.4.1.2. Con: Attempting to fix the prison system is always valid, even if there is little precedent for success in doing so. Success would benefit everybody enormously.
1.4.1.3. Pro: Prisons exist to serve a [variety of purposes](https://www.britannica.com/topic/prison#ref272178)(There are a number of accepted reasons for the use of imprisonment. One approach aims to deter those who would otherwise commit crimes \(general deterrence\) and to make it less likely that those who serve a prison sentence will commit crimes after their release \(individual deterrence\). A second approach focuses on issuing punishment to, or obtaining retribution from, those who have committed serious crimes. A third approach encourages the personal reform of those who are sent to prison. Finally, in some cases it is necessary to protect the public from those who commit crimes—particularly from those who do so persistently. In individual cases, all or some of these justifications may apply. The increasing importance of the notion of reform has led some prison systems to be called correctional institutions.) depending on the incarcerated individual: retribution, incapacitation, specific or individual deterrence, and rehabilitation.
1.4.1.3.1. Pro: In order for the system to have a chance to function properly, all stated purposes of imprisonment must be accounted for, at least to some degree.
1.4.1.3.2. Con: -> See 1.3.5.2.2.2.
1.4.1.4. Pro: Prisons should increasingly focus on rehabilitation as prisoners get closer to their release date.
1.4.1.4.1. Con: Rehabilitation and reintegration measures should start earlier, rather than later.
1.4.1.4.1.1. Pro: Properly training prisoners for any skilled job in a short time frame before being released would be difficult. Instead, they should be educated and trained in preparation for future employment throughout their incarceration.
1.4.1.4.1.2. Con: Beginning rehabilitation at the start of someone’s sentence is a waste of time if their sentence is longer than twenty years.
1.4.1.4.1.2.1. Pro: Some criminals are sentenced to life imprisonment, with no chance of parole. Rehabilitation for them would be a waste of time and effort.
1.4.1.4.1.2.1.1. Con: If criminals locked up on life sentences are deemed incapable of reform, then prisons may become hotbeds of depression and abuse as correctional officers \(and the wider community\) may consider them exiles and unworthy of compassion.
1.4.1.4.1.2.2. Con: Long sentences can be shortened, [depending on behavior](https://www.lawinfo.com/resources/criminal-defense/sentencing/what-is-good-behavior-in-prison.html)(It can be difficult for an inmate to follow all the rules and participate in programs while imprisoned. One of the ways jails and prisons keep prisoners participating is to reward them with benefits for “good behavior." Good behavior generally means following the rules and not getting in trouble. Good behavior credit can actually reduce the sentence by up to 54 days every year in some jurisdictions.\n\nGood time credit is available for individuals serving a sentence in federal prison. Many states have a similar policy. Understanding the good behavior policies and figuring out how to follow the rules can make a big difference in how you can get out of jail early.). Focusing on rehabilitation would ensure that these prisoners will be equipped to enter society upon release.
1.4.1.4.2. Pro: Rehabilitation while on probation is already a [standard practice](https://www.hg.org/legal-articles/court-ordered-rehab-and-the-consequences-for-violations-50649)(When a person commits a crime, there are several possible outcomes such as a conviction, a program, restitution and counseling. For some, a drug rehabilitation program is an alternative sentence when convicted of certain crimes. When sentenced to this rather than jail or prison, it is because the involved judge believes that it is better to keep the person away from prison or jail and serve a long-term rehabilitation instead of the standard incarceration period. If the convicted individual is a first-time offender, a peaceful and non-violent convict or someone with a minor conviction, it is possible to receive this sentence.) employed by courts for many first-time offenders.
1.4.1.4.3. Pro: Rehabilitation-focused systems can optimise for time and focus on the probability of a successful reintegration into society.
1.4.1.4.3.1. Pro: By analyzing each case individually, customized rehabilitation terms would reduce the probability of judicial bias.
1.4.1.4.3.2. Con: A system where prisoners are released when they are rehabilitated may result in reformed criminals who committed severe crimes being released sooner than unreformed criminals who engaged in petty criminality.
1.4.1.4.3.3. Pro: The earlier a prisoner is [released back into society](https://www.brennancenter.org/our-work/analysis-opinion/breaking-cycle-mass-incarceration)(One example of this is the reduc­tion in federal drug sentences. The U.S. Senten­cing Commis­sion reduced all federal crack cocaine sentences in 2007 and made the changes retro­act­ive, so that people serving those sentences could peti­tion to get the new reduced sentence. At the time, crit­ics \(includ­ing those at DOJ\) warned that this would be a public safety disaster because these people would be back on the streets too early. But the agency model allowed the commis­sion’s decision to proceed without being blocked.\n\nWhen the commis­sion stud­ied that popu­la­tion five years later, it found that the recidiv­ism rate of those released earlier was lower than the recidiv­ism rate of those who served their full sentences. That evid­ence, in turn, paved the way for the commis­sion in 2013 to reduce all federal drug sentences retro­act­ively, not just sentences for crack. So agen­cies end up making the case for their policies because policies groun­ded in evid­ence work more effect­ively.), the better their chances of avoiding a relapse and returning to prison.
1.4.1.4.3.4. Pro: A focus on ensuring reintegration into society will make false imprisonment less burdensome. Assuming the prisoner had never committed a crime before, the process would likely move along quickly.
1.4.1.4.3.4.1. Con: Many prisons do not allow inmates to engage in rehabilitation programs [unless they admit their guilt](https://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/home-news/wrongly-convicted-men-launch-new-case-against-the-justice-secretary-9985773.html)(If you don’t co-operate with the prison system, you are a nonentity. It tries to force and compel you to do rehabilitation work. If I was to do it I would have to admit my guilt, and I wouldn’t do that. My resolve was to clear my name. I don’t want to let this affect me for the rest of my life. I’m living below the poverty line, but I have a degree of optimism because I have a case worth fighting and I’m not going to give up. I’ll fight as long as it needs to be fought.). People who are unjustly imprisoned may refuse to do so out of principle.
1.4.1.5. Con: Rehabilitation is a worthy goal, but being within prison walls is not conducive to building life-changing behaviors.
1.4.1.5.1. Pro: When isolated from society in a concentrated population of other criminals, offenders cannot observe and practice positive interaction with society.
1.4.1.5.1.1. Con: People confined to prisons are part of society and are entitled to be protected from harm, even if safety concerns justify their confinement.
1.4.1.5.1.2. Pro: Prisoners need to understand how to behave in typical social and work settings, which requires being able to interact daily with common citizens, not fellow prisoners.
1.4.1.5.2. Pro: Many prisoners suffer from [depression and anxiety](https://www.news-medical.net/health/Prisoner-Anxiety.aspx)(Anxiety is a feeling of worry, nervousness, or fear that often comes when a person’s body responds to a critical or frightening situation. In this condition, the body prepares for an action, i.e., to fight against the threat or run away from it. When our mind prepares to cope with the threat, our body shows some physical symptoms, which vary based on the type of anxiety.\n\nThis is a common problem in people, especially among those sentenced to imprisonment. A study by the Ministry of Justice found that 23% of male and 49% of female prisoners suffer from depression and anxiety.); positive behavioural change is difficult to sustain under mental stress.
1.4.1.5.3. Con: Prison is an appropriate setting for some types of rehabilitation. Helping prisoners [gain marketable skills](https://www.theguardian.com/sustainable-business/2015/dec/09/prison-work-program-ohsa-whole-foods-inmate-labor-incarceration)(That incarcerated offenders often participate in prison work programs is nothing new. Today, however, this employment has taken on unprecedented scope, with computer coding and skilled manufacturing joining more traditional labor. Those who run these programs say the training they offer is essential for preparing prisoners to succeed in the outside world after release.) and practice positive behaviours can be carried out well in a prison context.
1.4.1.5.3.1. Pro: Rehabilitating a prisoner with viable job skills [reduces recidivism](https://www.justice.gov/archives/prison-reform)(Research shows that inmates who participate in correctional education programs have 43 percent lower odds of returning to prison than those who do not, and that every dollar spent on prison education saves four to five dollars on the costs of re-incarceration. With guidance from the Bronner Group, an educational consulting firm, BOP is building a semi-autonomous school district within the federal prison system and will offer programs for adult literacy/basic skills, high school diplomas, post-secondary education, and expanded opportunities for individuals with learning disabilities.) and provides a benefit to society when the prisoner is released.
1.4.1.5.3.1.1. Pro: Prison labor, or employing prisoners to work in certain industries, can help [reduce recidivism](https://www.theguardian.com/sustainable-business/2015/dec/09/prison-work-program-ohsa-whole-foods-inmate-labor-incarceration)(Among those with knowledge of the prison work system, it is generally believed that work experience helps reduce recidivism rates; several states, including Florida, California and Washington, have numbers showing that their program graduates a far less likely than average to reoffend.).
1.4.1.5.3.1.1.1. Pro: Prison labor helps prisoners develop job skills that can help them gain employment upon release, [aiding their reintegration](https://www.unodc.org/dohadeclaration/en/news/2019/03/teaching-prisoners-practical-work-skills-for-a-new-chance-in-life.html)(After they have paid their debt to society, the ability of ex-prisoners to find work is undoubtedly a factor which contributes significantly to their dignity and self-reliance, their sense of belonging, and the diminished likelihood of their recidivism.\n\[...\]\nThey will teach prisoners a variety of useful vocations, and help them improve their self-esteem, decision-making capacities, and confidence in their future. Importantly, these vocations will increase their employability and offer them better work prospects, or better entrepreneurial skills to support them in the case of self-employment. Enabling their smooth transition from incarceration to freedom, prison administrations provide convicts with external certification for their practical training, with documents which do not indicate that the skills were learned in prison.) into society.
1.4.1.5.3.1.1.2. Con: Prison labor is not the same as a clearly effective rehabilitation program; workers are frequently treated poorly and earn [pennies on the dollar](https://www.npr.org/transcripts/884989263)(And it was at the Omaha Correctional Center that they were hired for their first job working in the prison kitchens.\n\nMORGAN: My day would start at 4 a.m. I would go into the kitchen. I would make the breakfast for 1,200 men. I would work lunch. I would work dinner. And I'd make $2.25 a day.\n\nGARCIA: A day.\n\nMORGAN: A day.\n\nRAFIEYAN: That's $2.25 for more than 12 hours of work. After taxes, Dominique says they would take home about $54 a month) or [nothing at all](https://www.theguardian.com/sustainable-business/2015/dec/09/prison-work-program-ohsa-whole-foods-inmate-labor-incarceration)(The vast majority of working inmates are employed in support roles within the prison: washing dishes, doing laundry, delivering mail. The wages for these jobs are a fraction of what similar work would earn outside of prison; in the federal prison system, for example, the pay range is between $0.12 and $0.40 per hour. A few states do not require prisoners to be paid at all.), making prison labor [akin to modern slavery](https://www.freedomunited.org/prison-labor-and-modern-slavery/)(The line between free prison labor and forced prison labor is difficult to define. The International Labour Organization \(ILO\) lists several indicators of free prison labor which, if absent, could point to conditions of modern slavery. These include the right to written consent forms, wages and working hours comparable to those of free workers, and standard health and safety measures. The ILO states that these factors must be considered “as a whole” to determine if prison labor is forced or voluntary.\n\nThe United Nations Office on Drugs and Crime \(UNODC\) discusses prison labor in its so-called Nelson Mandela Rules, which outline minimum standards by which to treat those incarcerated; rule 97 states that those incarcerated “shall not be held in slavery of servitude” and that they must be covered by the same wage, health, and safety standards as free citizens.).
1.4.1.5.3.1.1.2.1. Con: Inmates can [save their earnings](https://www.npr.org/sections/thesalt/2015/09/30/444797169/whole-foods-says-it-will-stop-selling-foods-made-by-prisoners)(Scaggs says the inmates make about $1,500 to $2,500 a year, but he isn't sure what the hourly rate of pay is.\n\n"If an inmate is serving a sentence for a few years, they can come out with a few thousand bucks \[in savings\] and a whole new skill set," he says.) from their prison jobs to help them get back on their feet after they get released.
1.4.1.5.3.1.1.2.1.1. Con: Inmates can lose [up to 80%](https://www.theguardian.com/sustainable-business/2015/dec/09/prison-work-program-ohsa-whole-foods-inmate-labor-incarceration)(Up to 80% of inmates’ earnings, however, can be garnished to go toward room and board, victim restitution, child support and mandatory savings.) of their already low earnings due to victim restitution and child support payments.
1.4.1.5.3.1.1.2.1.2. Con: The [cost of legal goods and services](https://www.npr.org/transcripts/884989263)(After taxes, Dominique says they would take home about $54 a month, and most of that money went towards paying for things like phone calls, which could cost upwards of $5 per call or items from the canteen, like a bag of Doritos, which might be $5. Dominique says they might spend an entire day's pay on one stick of deodorant.\n\nGARCIA: And that's the case with many incarcerated people whose wages tend to get eaten up by paying for simple services - services that are often provided by private for-profit companies who split the revenue with the prison itself.) in prisons can be so astronomically high that nothing remains at the end of the month, let alone the end of their prison sentence.
1.4.1.5.3.1.1.3. Con: Many of the inmates that participate in prison labor schemes are [highly motivated individuals](https://www.theguardian.com/sustainable-business/2015/dec/09/prison-work-program-ohsa-whole-foods-inmate-labor-incarceration)(It is important to note, however, that participation in correctional industries programs is usually limited to the most trustworthy and motivated inmates, so it is hard to be sure exactly what factors lead to these lower recidivism rates. “You have a chicken-and-egg problem with recidivism studies,” Schrwatzapfel said.) and by their nature are less likely to reoffend.
1.4.1.5.3.1.1.4. Pro: Prison labor programs are a form of rehabilitative imprisonment; prisoners are [taught teamwork](https://www.npr.org/sections/thesalt/2015/09/30/444797169/whole-foods-says-it-will-stop-selling-foods-made-by-prisoners)("This is a model example of a prison-work program," Scaggs says. "By purchasing goat's milk from the facility \[that uses prison labor\], we're supporting ... rehabilitative incarceration." He says prisoners are taught teamwork and getting job training.) and the on-the-job training provides them with skills for reintegration into society.
1.4.1.5.3.1.2. Con: Many businesses are simply [opposed to hiring felons](https://www.felonyrecordhub.com/dont-companies-hire-felons/)(Many employers won’t hire felons, believing they are dishonest and likely to commit a crime on the job. Or employers fear the public finding out they hire felons, damaging the company’s reputation and losing business.\n\nAnother reason is to protect their company. If they hire a felon who causes problems on the job, it could be viewed as negligent hiring. This might allow safety factors at work to place the business at risk and jeopardize their standing with the public.\n\nAnother issue is the stereotype: once a felon always a felon. These employers believe that a felon can’t change and will always resort back to crime at some point.), preventing skilled and experienced ex-convicts from being hired.
1.4.1.5.3.1.2.1. Con: The problem of businesses refusing to hire ex-convicts is a problem that can be solved or mitigated for the good of ex-convicts and society, not a reason to give up on rehabilitation.
1.4.1.5.3.1.3. Con: Not all criminals committed their offences because they could not get a job. Job training will not help these prisoners.
1.4.1.5.3.1.3.1. Pro: [White-collar criminals](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/w/white-collar-crime.asp)(White-collar crime has been associated with the educated and affluent ever since the term was first coined in 1949 by sociologist Edwin Sutherland, who defined it as "crime committed by a person of respectability and high social status in the course of his occupation."2 White-collar workers historically have been the "shirt and tie" set, defined by office jobs and management, and not "getting their hands dirty." This class of worker stands in contrast to blue-collar workers, who traditionally wore blue shirts and worked in plants, mills, and factories.) are unlikely to benefit from job skills training, as a lack of a job was not the cause of their criminality in the first place.
1.4.1.5.3.1.3.1.1. Con: White-collar criminals should not be the focus of rehabilitation programs because they are enabled by their position of power. In order to prevent recidivism among this group, their power merely needs to be taken away.
1.4.1.5.3.1.3.2. Con: In one study, only [49% of prisoners](https://www.brookings.edu/blog/up-front/2018/03/14/5-facts-about-prisoners-and-work-before-and-after-incarceration/)(2. But prisoners fared poorly in the labor market before they were incarcerated. Of the prisoners we studied, only 49 percent of prime-age men were employed two full calendar years prior to incarceration. Of those who were employed, their median earnings were only $6,250. Only 13 percent earned more than $15,000. Indeed in any given year in the decade prior to their incarceration, only about half of prisoners had any reported earnings.) surveyed were in secure employment before their offense.
1.4.1.5.3.1.3.3. Pro: Many crimes, such as sexual offenses, are not motivated by economic factors, so job skills training would not rehabilitate these types of offenders.
1.4.1.5.3.2. Con: Suitable learning environments [make the learner feel safe](https://raccoongang.com/blog/what-makes-good-learning-environment/)(A good learning environment offers a safe platform for learners. Before you can expect learners to succeed academically, they should also feel safe both mentally and physically. While most of the schools take physical safety measures, not many learning platforms consider the mental safety of the learners.\n\nSafety in a learning environment goes beyond physical well-being. In order to maintain a safe learning environment, learners must feel supported, welcomed, and respected.), both physically and mentally; inmates are not likely to feel either in prison environments.
1.4.1.5.3.3. Con: Having a marketable skill does not immediately equate to being able to earn gainful employment.
1.4.1.5.3.3.1. Pro: People need to learn to how to market their skills, which requires social interaction.
1.4.1.5.3.4. Pro: For those who turned to crime due to a lack of education, a rough upbringing, and poverty, developing marketable skills through rehabilitation can be a way to break free from the cycle of violence.
1.4.1.5.3.4.1. Pro: Many non-profit organizations are dedicated to breaking the cyclical relationship between youth crime and poor education, and there have been some remarkable [success stories](https://www.teachfirst.org.uk/stories/breaking-cycle-youth-crime-involves-learning-not-prison-cells)(Whatever It Takes \(WIT\) offers a radical blueprint for the way we work with children and young people caught up in the destructive cycle of crime. Currently in its pilot stage, the charity provides children and young people \(who are or are at risk of repeatedly committing crimes\) with the support of a WIT Guide. These professionals will have the time, skills, experience and support to do whatever it takes to get alongside each child - helping them to change their lives so that, one day, their services will no longer be needed.).
1.4.1.5.3.5. Pro: Criminal rehabilitation can be administered as [a form of therapy](https://www.dailyscandinavian.com/prison-life-in-scandinavia/)(Based on figures, it’s safe to assume Norway’s criminal justice system is doing something right. Few citizens there go to prison, and those who do usually go only once. So how does Scandinavia accomplish this feat? The countries rely on a concept called “restorative justice,” which aims to repair the harm caused by crime rather than punish people.), as it is in Norway and elsewhere. Therapy in itself is not a form of punishment; even when court-ordered and enforced, it doesn't have to be unpleasant.

Pro: [In the Soviet Union](https://www.france24.com/en/20190313-russias-powerful-thieves-law-face-reckoning-under-putin)(The 'Thieves in Law' emerged in the Soviet-era gulags, controlling the criminal underworld in Stalin's prison camps.\n\nThey developed their own subculture and jargon, similar to Italy's Cosa Nostra or the Yakuza in Japan, and were both lionised and feared.\n\nThey flourished in the chaos that followed the 1991 collapse of the Soviet Union, building huge criminal empires and branching out across Europe, the Middle East and North America.), entire criminal subcultures developed in prison camps.
1.4.4.1.5.2. Pro: Correctional officers in primarily punishment-based facilities could be more inclined to [abuse their position](https://www.theguardian.com/society/2019/apr/29/more-than-2500-prison-staff-disciplined-in-five-years-moj-figures-show)(More than 2,500 prison staff have been subject to disciplinary action in the last five years, including for relationships with inmates, assaulting prisoners and racism, the Guardian can reveal, as the government prepares to launch a unit to tackle corruption in jails.\n\nA total of 2,666 prison staff in England and Wales were subject to disciplinary action between mid-2013 and mid-2018, according to data released by the Ministry of Justice \(MoJ\) under the Freedom of Information Act.) and harm the prisoners.
1.4.4.1.5.2.1. Pro: Guards abusing their positions of power would [constitute a crime](https://www.thewestmorlandgazette.co.uk/news/19897564.hmp-haverigg-prison-officer-arrested-suspected-misconduct/)(A PRISON worker at HMP Haverigg has been arrested by organised crime officers.\n\nThe 49-year-old woman from Millom was arrested on suspicion of misconduct in a public office, the North West Regional Organised Crime Unit said.\n\nShe was taken to a police station for questioning having been arrested by officers from the Regional Prison Intelligence Unit yesterday.\n\nShe has since been released under investigation.), increasing the crime rate.
1.4.4.1.5.2.2. Pro: Guards abusing their position could leave prisoners disillusioned on release, resulting in a higher crime rate.
1.4.4.1.5.2.3. Con: [Abuse of power](https://www.theguardian.com/society/2020/apr/30/uk-prison-officers-punching-inmates)(Prison officers are allegedly punching compliant inmates who they suspect might misbehave in the future in a practice known as “preventive strikes”, a European human rights watchdog has said in a damning report on the state of jails in England.\n\nA delegation from the European Committee for the Prevention of Torture \(CPT\), part of the human rights organisation the Council of Europe, inspected three local male prisons – Liverpool, Wormwood Scrubs in west London and Doncaster – in May last year.) is rampant in the [prison system](https://www.themarshallproject.org/2015/12/20/spotting-the-red-flags-of-abusive-prison-guards)(The judge, John Gleeson of the Eastern District of New York, noted there were “red flags all over the employment history” of the guard, who had been the target of numerous other abuse claims brought by inmates, according to a transcript of his remarks. But the complaints, which were handwritten, were filed internally and “buried in the prison’s arcane filing system,” which organizes claims by inmate identification numbers, not the officers involved, Gleeson said.\n\nIt was a startling observation — that prison officials had no way of tracking problems with guards. But lawyers and prisoners’ rights advocates have long complained about the issue throughout the state prison system), even with rules in place to prevent it. There is no reason to assume that rehabilitation would be different.
1.4.4.1.5.2.3.1. Con: Since retributive justice systems are based on the principle of harming those who have broken society’s rules, they are more inclined to lead to abuses of power.
1.4.4.1.5.2.3.2. Con: A general pivot towards rehabilitation would encourage more social workers and mental health specialists to work in prisons. The presence of these professionals, coupled with the retraining of correctional officers, should reduce the risk of abuse.
1.4.4.1.5.2.3.2.1. Pro: Prison staff could be retrained to become rehabilitators with experience and expertise in pedagogy, psychology, and dynamic security \([p. 5](https://www.epta.info/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/EPTA_05_SIG2-Dynamic-Security_EN_def.pdf)(Dynamic security is a concept and a working method by which staff prioritizing the creation and maintenance of everyday communication and interaction with\ninmates based on high professional ethics, and that there is sufficient purposeful and meaningful activity to occupy prisoners, bound by effective security. It aims to better\nunderstand inmates and assess the risks they may pose as well as ensuring safety, security and good order, contributing to rehabilitation and preparation for release. This concept should be understood within a broader notion of security which also comprises structural, organisational and static security.)\).
1.4.4.1.5.2.3.2.1.1. Con: In general, training in prisons is inadequate. Retraining prison guards to become mental health professionals is unlikely to be a successful endeavor \([p. 1](https://www.urban.org/sites/default/files/publication/63191/900718-Prisoner-Abuse-Is-Avoidable.pdf)(Another ticket to abuse is reliance on administrators and troops largely untrained in prison management.)\).
1.4.4.1.5.2.3.3. Pro: Disinterested and antagonistic correctional officers are partly responsible for failed rehabilitation programs in the UK \([p. 111](https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/pdf/10.1177/1748895818800743)(Rehabilitative interventions – notably Offender Management Programmes \(OMPS\) and work placements – are perceived to be self-serving in rationale. They are experienced as ill-resourced, superficial in approach and unlikely to engender change. The prison climate, characterized by a lack of interest among correctional staff, lack of empathy and concern, and mixed – but often impersonal and sometimes antagonistic – relationships between prison staff and prisoners, further disrupts any ethos of rehabilitation.)\).
1.4.4.1.5.3. Pro: -> See 1.3.3.4.1.
1.4.4.1.5.4. Con: The criminal subculture of street gangs could easily create the same conditions for worsening social compatibility; this issue is not unique to prisons.
1.4.4.2. Con: -> See 1.2.1.4.2.
1.4.4.3. Con: As [criminality is pervasive](https://www.psychologytoday.com/us/blog/crimes-courts-and-cops/201001/everyones-criminal)(That although we like to think of criminals as being very different from us, they're actually not. Of course, I am not saying that you are a murderer or rapist \(although, maybe you are\); but often the difference between us and someone with a criminal record is one of degree or chance. Let me explain.\n\nFirst, a definition from the American Heritage Dictionary: Criminal - n. A person who has committed or been legally convicted of a crime. Notice the disjunctive; you are a criminal if you have violated a criminal law, regardless of whether you were convicted. And keep in mind that criminal laws are incredibly broad in their applicability.) in society, locking up enough people to ensure the safety of others may result in most of the global population being imprisoned.
1.4.4.4. Pro: Career criminals find it much more difficult to [organise criminal enterprises while imprisoned](https://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/politics/crime-bosses-prison-jail-crackdown-cut-off-high-security-new-plans-justice-secretary-david-gauke-a8241221.html)(Crime bosses who run gangland empires from their prison cell will be cut off from the outside world in high-security jails, under a new crackdown.\n\nThe Justice Secretary will outline plans to expand the criteria for locking up offenders in the most tightly controlled jails, to include the likelihood of them pursuing criminal activity while inside.\n\n\[...\]\n\nThe more secure prisons involve higher and more dangerous walls, double internal fencing with motion detectors, 24/7 dog patrols, enhanced intelligence staffing, embedded counter-terror units, armoured vehicles and technology to disrupt mobile signals.\n\nThere are onsite hospitals to mitigate the need for any prisoner to leave, as well helicopter wire to stop aircraft approaching.\n\nCritically, those going in and out of the jails are subject to much stricter searches, including rub downs and a full-body scanner to identify objects hidden internally.).
1.4.4.4.1. Con: Some crime bosses have still managed to run their empires [while incarcerated](https://www.nationalcrimeagency.gov.uk/news/71-years-for-cocaine-trafficking-group-led-from-prison)(71 years for cocaine trafficking group led from prison\n\nA convicted drug dealer who continued to run his criminal enterprise from prison has been jailed for 25 years alongside associates who helped him fix deals on the outside and launder the profits.).
1.4.4.4.2. Pro: Depending on the nature of the criminal organisation, arresting key figures may result in a decline in crime rates \([p. 10](https://docs.iza.org/dp12534.pdf)(In hierarchical structures like the Mafia, removing a single leader may have little impact on overall crime, since there is always a lower-level leader in place who is ready to move up in the hierarchy. The Chicago Mafia, for example, continued their operations after their most notorious leader, Al Capone, was put in prison in 1932. \n\[...\]\nIt is important to keep in mind, however, that most organized crime structures are not as hierarchical as the Mafia. In fact, many can be described as loose associations of independent contractors or brokers, each with their own specific skills or resources. These brokers work in a chain-like crime production system that produces a well-defined product. Arresting one key link can break this chain and lower crime.)\).
1.4.4.5. Pro: Every criminal that gets re-arrested after being released from prison provides proof that crimes were prevented while they were locked up.
1.4.4.5.1. Con: Criminals that never reoffend are examples of crime not being prevented simply because someone is behind bars.
1.4.4.6. Con: Separating criminals from would-be victims by putting them in prison prevents crime regardless of whether the focus of the prison is rehabilitative or for punishment.
1.4.5. Con: Being separated from society is not necessarily a punishment.
1.4.5.1. Pro: If deprivation of liberty were a punishment, military personnel would experience punishment most of the time, except when they are on leave.
1.4.5.1.1. Con: Joining the military to avoid imprisonment is [occasionally suggested](https://www.stripes.com/news/judge-said-army-or-jail-but-military-doesn-t-want-him-1.44417)(The judge is willing to give him a conditional discharge Bookstaver said. One of the conditions of that discharge, in a sense, is that he follow through on his own desire to join the military.\n\nHe said the judge did not want to sentence Guerra to probation because her understanding is that would preclude him from joining the Army.) by judges, suggesting that the military is akin to punishment.
1.4.5.2. Con: Exile, the most extreme form of separation from society, was long considered the only [alternative to the death penalty](https://www.britannica.com/topic/exile-law)(In Rome, exile \(exsilium\) arose as a means of circumventing the death penalty \(see capital punishment\). Before a death sentence was pronounced, a Roman citizen could escape by voluntary exile. Later, degrees of exile were introduced, including temporary or permanent exile, exile with or without loss of citizenship, and exile with or without confiscation of property. The Romans generally determined punishment by class, applying sentences of banishment to the upper classes and sentences of forced labour to the lower classes.) for certain crimes.
1.4.5.2.1. Pro: Some US states still allow people to be [banished to other states](https://thehill.com/opinion/criminal-justice/423018-bring-back-exile-as-a-sentencing-option/)(Justices in Georgia, Mississippi, Arkansas, Florida and Kentucky still have intra-state exile as options during sentencing. Although the Court of Appeals for Mississippi has said exile must reasonably resemble the goals of probation including rehabilitation and the defendant’s constitutional rights must not be violated. Some states find that exile benefits a criminal by separating them from association with other criminal friends in their home county. Exile is also still used by various Native American tribes to banish criminals from the reservations.) as a form of punishment.
1.4.5.3. Pro: Many individuals and groups of people function separately from modern society.
1.4.5.3.1. Con: Separation, in the context of prison, is the forcible involuntary exclusion from society as a punishment. Someone who has deliberately chosen to leave society is able to return, while prisoners do not have that option.
1.4.5.3.2. Pro: Some individuals willingly separate themselves from society, preferring to live "[off the grid](https://morningchores.com/living-off-the-grid/)(In its most basic form, living “off-grid” means your home isn’t connected to a local power grid. If the power you use in your home is from a municipal power hub, then you’re on the grid.\n\nYou have to pay money to use grid power and there’s an outage at the main hub \(or if your local lines are damaged\), your power will go down. This can cause a few hours of inconvenience or it can mean several days without water for rural folks whose pumps are powered by electricity.\n\nWhen you’re living off-grid, it means that you either have a power source of your own, or you’re living without electricity. You might also be disconnected from other municipal lines such as water and sewage. Maybe you have an outhouse and a well, or a septic tank and a rainwater catchment system.)."
1.4.5.3.3. Pro: Mental hospitals, retreats, communes, boarding schools, monasteries, and others separate people from society. These institutions are highly valued by many and are not considered to be a punishment.
1.4.5.3.3.1. Con: A key difference is whether or not the subject is being removed from society willingly. In general, people go to monasteries voluntarily, but not to mental hospitals.
1.4.5.3.3.2. Con: Some institutions designed to separate their inhabitants from society can have [long-term detrimental](https://www.theguardian.com/books/2015/jun/08/boarding-school-syndrome-joy-schaverien-review)(I once knew an American psychoanalyst who worked in a Bangkok practice, specialising in expats. He’d first come to east Asia on contract for an international church whose missionaries kept getting into trouble. He never went home: there was more than enough work. “Specifically,” he said, “with people like you. Middle-aged, middle-class Brits who went to your crazy private schools may just about be the most damaged social sub-group I’ve ever come across.”\n\nIt’s long been known that the practice of sending young children off into the care of strangers is not wholly safe. The ancient “public” schools worried the Victorians as much or more than did the workhouses: three parliamentary commissions sat in the 19th century to look at the financial frauds, riots and the astonishing numbers of deaths – from suicides, assaults by teachers and pupils, starvation, epidemics – in the schools of the rich. Tom Brown’s Schooldays only scraped the surface.) impacts on those who attend them.
1.4.5.3.3.3. Pro: Drug rehab facilities and psychiatric hospitals isolate people, allowing them to undergo treatment and rehabilitation without risking harm to the public or themselves.
1.4.5.4. Con: Criminals are punished by the act of being imprisoned. The removal of one's liberty and loss of time is the ultimate punishment.
1.4.5.4.1. Con: Prison and the deprivation of freedom themselves are not considered a penance. Penance is what one does to atone for their crimes or infractions to try to repair the damage.
1.4.5.4.1.1. Con: Prison sentences reflect the debt owed to society for violating its laws and have nothing to do with penance or restitution to the victim.
1.4.5.4.1.1.1. Pro: Convicts are free to pursue penance and/or forgiveness from the victim once their debt to society has been repaid.
1.4.5.4.1.2. Pro: Prisons prevent inmates from repairing any damage caused and only delay their penance. The deprivation of freedom also prevents them from learning from their mistakes.
1.4.5.5. Pro: Separation from society is only considered a punishment if the separation is [deliberately intended to punish](https://parentingbeyondpunishment.com/separation-discipline-macnamara/)(The one theme that pervades disciplinary approaches used in homes and schools today is that they are separation based by design. Separation-based discipline refers to the removal of something a child cares about for the purpose of getting compliance and to reduce certain types of behaviour.\n\nSeparation based discipline comes in many forms but is commonly referred to as time-outs, 123 magic, pretending to leave a child when they don’t follow, taking away things they care about until they comply, ignoring and giving them the ‘cold shoulder’, punishing them with spanking, or shaming them in front of others. They are other forms of separation-based discipline, but the underlying theme is the same – if you misbehave you will face separation from the things you care about.\n\nThe hope in using this approach is that the pain inflicted will teach a child a lesson they will remember the next time they act.) an individual or group.
